SATURDAY, MARCH 5, 1892.
IOU.
To him who waits everything comes at last,
and after long waiting the end of the
great borrowing policy has practically come
for the provinces of Australasia. It hasarrivcd
unexpectedly and unaccountably, and to the
politicians whose one and only idea of states-
manship has, for thirty years past, been loans
and still more loans, the situation is per-
plexing. The idea which they all along
entertained was that their country could
borrow so long as its securities lasted, and
the possibility of the source 1 of supply drying
up seemed an event too remote to be seri-
ously contemplated. Yet the source has dried
up —the financial Colossus has crumbled
there is no more available loose change in
Britain, and the pawnbroking industry is as
nearly as possible hopeless. Australia’s
security is still the best in-the world—
better even than that of Britain or the
United States, which have nothing to show
for their financial burdens save old wars,
more or less disastrous—incalculably better
than that of Continental Europe, where, by
reason of the bloated armaments, every great
State has a huge and ever-growing deficiency.
.But the old loan-mongering firms and syndi-
cates through which the financial industry
was conducted have mostly come to grief,
and those that survive are weak and shaky.
Even the Bank of England was saved only
recently through French assistance, and its
present condition causes so much alarm that
the British authorities are considering
revolutionary devices for putting it on a
safer footing. Consols are kept up merely
by a system of forced currency ; all trust-
moneys are required by law to be invested
either in these, in Bank of England stock,
or in Indian Government securities, and
but for this, the bonds of the Empire
would fall with a crash into the region of
almost unmarketable paper. Britain has no
longer any money to lend, and the three-ball
business is closed up for the present through
the financial embarrassments of the great
national Hebrew, whose gilded sign has hung
out so long over the North Sea inviting all
and sundry to come and put the earth up
the spout.
* * *
No one else shows an inclination to take
over the business at this crisis. England is
by no means the richest land on earth—
both the United States and France have
probably more available gold to lend, but no
other country has ever shown any serious
desire to compete with it in the great bill-
discounting industry. England itself never
really possessed more than enough wealth
for its own internal necessities, but through
the concentration of property in the hands
of a very few, and the abject pauperisation
of the rest of the population, the rich
were ajfle to lend out at interest the money
which should have bought tables and chairs
for the poor, and to finance half the world
by dint of leaving the labourer’s roof un-
repaired and his children hungry and shoe-
less. And now the shrinkage of Britain’s
commerce, the hopeless decline of land-
values, the ruin of agriculture, and the col-
lapse one after another of many foreign
debtors have dried up her available
resources. And this complication of dis-
asters has arrived at an unfortunate season
for Australia. In Victoria and New South
Wales the earlier loans were mostly floated
for terms of twenty-five or thirty years.
They were raised with a joyous disregard of
the future, so long as the men of that day
could spend the money and stave off the
payment till the next generation. Presently
borrowing became a fixed habit, like alcohol
or opium, and the country grew to depend
so much on this artificial stimulant that the
years when no loan was floated were always
years of depression and general wretched-
ness. The necessity for more borrowed
money is hoav as great as ever, but a double-
barrelled calamity has fallen upon Australia:
the new loans are practically unobtainable,
and the old ones are falling due. The
depression which has arisen in the past
through a temporary stoppage in the influx
of borrowed money is nothing to the depres-
sion which will exist in the future, when not
only is there no influx, but a steady outflow
arises in its stead—unless, indeed, some
vigorous and statesmanlike measures are
taken to meet the emergency.
• * *
The financial trouble of the present
moment is only the beginning of the
disaster. During the remaining eight
years of the century, Victoria and New
South Wales have each to liquidate some
£5,000,000 of their ancient obligations;
after that the old debentures of the two
provinces begin to fall in like an avalanche.
Of all the next 32 years there is hardly one
in which some venerable loan does not come
due, and even if the country can, for a time
at least, float new loans to liquidate these
old ones, and still newer loans to liquidate the
new ones when they grow old in their turn,
the prospect in view is only one long, drearv
panorama of profitless finance. The most
that can be hoped for, in the woeful and
seemingly final collapse of the great pawn-
broking business, is that the influx of
borrowed money is practically at an end, but
that the country may still raise enough, by
constant worry and difficulty, to stave off
each ancient debt a little while longer when
the implacable creditor presents the bill, and
then start staving them all off over again,
and so on ad infinitum ; the least that can bo
looked for is that Victoria and New South
Wales may have to pay off these old
liabilities out of their own resources through
32 years of care and hard economy. Pro-
bably the latter would be, in the end, the
more fortunate alternative. The time must
come when national repudiation will become
so general that no country will lend a single
copper on the faith of any Government, save
possibly its own, at any rate of interest
whatsoever, and there is very little to be
gained by postponing the difficulty a few
years longer.
* » •
Neither Victoria nor New South Wales,
however, shows any inclination to face the
problem. The two provinces have simul-
taneously struck the device of raising
money on short-dated Treasury bills at
an increased rate of interest, and these
securities will fall due some two or three
years hence, at a time when the other
financial troubles are sufficiently insuper-
able. The country is trusting entirely to
Providence for a means of meeting the
liabilities already in view, and the further
debts now being incurred are left to the
same beneficent agency. When a man
under sentence of death commits another
murder he probably does it on the ground
that he cannot well be hanged twice, and
this new system of finance is based appa-
rently on the same principle. It marks a
further decadence in the principles of finance.
The Treasurers of the past were content so
long as their debts could be hung over till
the times of the next generation ; the Trea-
surer of the present is satisfied if he can
postpone his problem till a date when some
other Treasurer will probably be in office.
By-and-bye the great ambition of the politi-
cian who holds the cash will be to keep
solvent for the current month, and let the
deluge come the month after. Then the
Government will trade on overdrafts payable
on demand, and will go round at night to
discount its joint and several P.N. with a
finance company, or will put the public
lands up the spout at the Monte de
Piete, and sneak away through back
streets with the ticket. The introduc-
tion of short-dated Treasury bills would
bo a step in the right direction, if it were
accompanied by some absolutely cast-iron
provision, whereby no more loans could be
raised in the British market, either now or
hereafter. Under such conditions, it would
signify a resolve to rely for the future on
our own resources ; without any such pro-
vision it is merely a device of shreds and
patches—a miserable scheme for letting the
future take care of itself at 4| per cent,
interest.
*# » (
The intense desire to avoid new taxation
is one of the chief reasons why no Treasurer,
either in Victoria or New South Wales, has
yet ventured to face the necessities of the
situation. A democracy is hardly ever pre-
pared to tax itself, however great the emer-
gency, and in this respect the conduct of the
people of Maoriland, during the last ten years
of rigid economy, is one of the marvels of
history. The Government which holds otlice
longest is always the one which borrows
most lavishly, constructs the most public
works, useless or otherwise, takes off the
most taxes, has the largest boom, and lets
the future take care of itself with the most
complete recklessness. The sure road to
popularity is a reduction of one farthing in
the £ on the property-tax, or the removal of
some small impost on some small edible ; the
loss to the revenue, is serious and no genuine
benefit is felt by anyone, but the mere fact
that something has been taken offis sufficient.
At the present time, strange as the statement
may appear, the Australians are among the
most lightly-taxed nations in the civilised
world. The revenues of these provinces are
largely made up from land sales and railway
receipts—these latter being inserted in gross,
in order to make up a bulky and purely arti-
ficial total. The municipal rates which in
England make up almost as great a burden
as the whole charges of the Imperial revenue
are here mostly included in the general
expenditure of the State, and the total direct
charge upon each citizen is not much more
than half that of Great Britain. But this
diminution of the so-called “public burdens”
does not necessarily produce prosperity ;
quite as often it is the cause of depression.
A heavily-taxed nation, where the money is
wisely expended, is in all cases much more
prosperous than a lightly-taxed one which
makes good the deficiency by loans and
similar shady devices. The demand for a
reduction of public burdens is a mere super-
stition, but the Treasurer who will openly
assert the fact and act upon his convictions
is yet unborn. Probably, regarded simply
as a Treasurer, he might as well remain so,
for the nation would reject him promptly
and with derision.
* * *
Yet the only way out of the present diffi-
culty is by taxation—or else repudiation.
The device of staving off the responsibility is
played out. If the present revenue is in-
sufficient—as it would seem to be—then a
new impost should be imposed—a land tax,
with a specially heavy charge upon big
estates, and a still more ponderous rate on
absentees, for preference—and the proceeds
paid into a special fund for the exclusive
purpose of meeting the national obligations
as they fall due. This fund should bo kept
entirely apart from the general revenue,
and it should be made a criminal act for
the Government to use it or any part of
ic for any purpose save that for which it
was intended. All new public works should
be built upon the “ betterment ” principle,
by levying upon those property-holders whose
estates are enhanced in value through such
works at least one-half of the profits which
they thereby receive, to cover the cost of con-
struction. If the sum thus obtained is not
sufficient for the purpose the balance should
be made good either out of general revenue,
or by the issue of redeemable notes of a
State bank, or by both devices combined.
Such a policy would probably put a sudden
and violent end to immigration and it would
be well that it should do so. It might even
cause a serious falling off in the city popula-
tion, for under this Spartan regime the great
army of land - boomers, brokers, money-
lenders, bagmen, jockeys, bookmakers,
company-promoters, rnine-salters, jerry-
builders, pawnbrokers, and the like would
be woefully thinned out; they all live on the
feverish and unhealthy prosperity that is
born of borrowed money and frantic extra-
vagance, and without it their respective
occupations would fall away. But their loss
would be a great gain to the rest of the
nation, and even a temporary decline in the
census-returns would not necessarily be a
cause for lamentation. And in any case the
problem must be met, for sooner or later-
most probably sooner—a choice will have to
be made between payment or repudiation.
Will any Treasurer have the courage to give
up the worn-out policy of letting things slide
till the morrow, and state which alternative
he proposes to accept ?
Trade-marks and Treason.
Reference was recently made by The Bulle-
tin to the prosecution of some Melbourne firms
for infringements of the law relating to trade-
marks. The reference did little more than
invite attention to a subject of cardinal moment
without exhaustively examining it. But the
matter is too important, especially to Austra-
lians, to be dismissed with a paragraph. We
therefore propose to examine it more fully,
although conscious, from the magnitude of the
question itself as well as from its intimate
connection with other questions of vital interest
to young countries, that how lengthily soever it
is treated much will still remain to be said,
• # #
The particulars are fresh in the public mind.
A Melbourne brewery company was found to be
adding to its sales and consequent profits by
the simple plan of pasting false labels on its
goods. The “lager” supposed to have seen
the light in Munich was cradled and nursed
into fermentation in Collingwood Flat. The
mistake was made at the christening, when it
was given a German name instead of a Victorian
one. It was a light, wholesome, sparkling
beer, probably as good as any brewed between
the Rhine and the Niemen ; but it certainly had
no right to the label it appropriated. Its
innocuousness and gently exhilarating qualities
no more entitled it to the name it filched than
the commonplace soldiership of honest dav
labourer John M‘Liver’s son entitled him t
the name of Colin Campbell. Still, there '°
this to be said in its behalf—it was drim/ 8
relished, and lauded by Anglo-Australians wh ’
drew unpleasant comparisons between it and
the poor stuff brewed by the Yarra. Jn
word, so far as the facts of the case have
to light, the Melbourne lager was receive 1
everywhere as a highly-creditable member 0 f
the beer family till the legitimacy of its birtl
was first doubted and then denied.
# # #
The other cases of pirated trade-marks were
in connection with hats of the pattern familiarly
known as “ plug ” or “ bell-topper.” For a lonv
time Paris has been famed for the manufacture
of these depressing adjuncts to modern civili sa .
tion. This is not the place to discuss the
characteristics of such articles of head-dress or
of the heads usually entrenched in them. It
is enough to state that from Paris and in a
lesser degree from London thousands of such
nightmares are exported yearly to gladden the
hearts and oppress the heads of loiterers on
fashionable Australian street-blocks, as well as to
be subsequently used by astute traders in con-
ferring dignity on South Sea Island monarchs
whose subjects’ allegiance shows signs of
wavering. The complaint of the Melbourne
Customs was that, in some instances brought to
their notice, the white internal leather bands
where the maker’s name could alone be seen
were imported stamped with the names of
European cities, while the hats to which such
bands were afterwards attached were manu-
factured in Collins-street., The object was
obvious—to persuade purchasers that it was
European, not Australian, articles they were buy-
ing. No question of quality or inequality was
involved. The local article might have
been superior to the foreign. The offence lay
in attaching to it a name to which it was not
entitled, just as large Sydney furniture-houses
offend against fair - dealing by representing
Chinese-made chairs to be of Caucasian work-
manship.
* * *
From a moral standpoint, the actions of the
detected Melbourne firms and of the undetected
Sydney ones, are equally indefensible and un-
worthy. No ingenuity of casuistry could make
them appear creditable. The men -who suc-
ceed in creating a demand for a commodity are
as much entitled to the trade-mark differentiat-
ing their goods from those of others as they are to
their purses; and the stranger using the brand for
the purpose of securing a market for similar
merchandise of even superior quality is at once
dishonest and mean. So well is this recognised
in some countries that but the other day a
couple of French brothers were prohibited
from using their own name on the champagne
made by them, another firm with a similar
patronymic having long previously established
a lucrative trade in the same article. In short
the right of a trader to his mark is the right of
an author to his book, a playwright to his play,
an inventor to his patent, or an artist to his
sketch. All the foregoing persons may have
their rights tampered with by unscrupulous
men. The dull British dramatist does not
scruple to filch from his livelier French confrere
the choicest situations or daintiest dialogues,
and vainly endeavours to pass them off as his
own, much as the school dunce “ cogs ” from
his class-mate’s slate, and occasionally imposes
on the teacher. But whether the victim be
tradesman, author, or artist, the offence com-
mitted against him is as dishonest as it is
scurvy.
» # •
Haying said so much regarding the meanness
of this prevalent commercial habit of endea-
vouring to reap where others have sown, it might
be as well to examine another and, perhaps,
more important phase of the same subject. This
wide-spread trading desire to “wipe the eye”
of one’s brother huckster, by appropriating his
symbols, is but a symptom of a dangerous
disease that has done and is doing the greatest
mischief to the whole Australian community.
To punish one or a hundred tradesmen for
obtaining money by false pretences, or trade-
marks dishonest!} 7 come by, will avail nothing
so long as the dangerous disease referred to is
suffered to run its course unchecked. As well
try to cure smallpox by removing some of the
pustules by the ungentle assistance of a jack-
plane. The deadly ailment to which reference
is made might not inaptly be termed Anglo, or
Franco, or Europo-mania, and manifests itself
chiefly in a stolid belief that nothing good can
come out of Australia. But for this irritatingly-
stupid creed, the Customs authorities in all the
Australian capitals might rest assured that the
person who brewed sparkling beer, or manu-
factured presentable hats, would have but little
temptation to introduce either under an assumed
name. The greenness of distant fields has
become a proverb in all languages, and, as we
shall proceed to show, it is precisely at this
point —at the carefully-nurtured scepticism
regarding Australia’s present and proximate
capabilities—that the curse of being '<ut a
colony comes home to all of us.
* # •
It is this senseless desire to go far afield in
search of commodities which could be produced
at our own doors that is largely responsible for
the low standard of Australian commercial
morality. It may be said that the same trait is
to be observed in American society—that
“ dudeism ” is but Anglomania in excelsis.
Still, dudeism itself is only a form of degeneracy
and is confined to a class which in the United
States is numerically and intellectually con-
temptible. The 400 New York aristocrats—
descendants of Dutch boors and British bag-
gages —of whom so much was heard a few years
ago, possess absolutely no weight in questions
affecting politics or progress. "So long as they
confine themselves to the latest things in boots,
bangles, or breeches from Paris or London,
they are suffered to proceed unmolested and
clap their hands exultingly But they are
rudely thrust aside or insultingly ignored when
matters of State, of science ov of sociology
have to be considered. The “Anglomania”
that many newspapers on both Atlantic littorals
assure us is rampant in America turns out on
examination to be confined to a small, rich,
ignorant and insignificant class. The vast body
of the nation remains unaffected by the disease


To Readers of “ The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains lias at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly or
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to Tint
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. Ii
certain of the so-called prize-competitions th»
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work tor him for noth
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1300 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.

°i ve f r om a subordinate Colonial Office clerk
to S ay that the Netherlands Government would
inquire into the affair, all the actors being dead.
4 law-suit, which has already half-ruined the
litigants, is sent for final revision and decision
to a, land in another hemisphere — a land pos-
sibly never visited by the disputants and cer-
tainly never seen by the bulk of Australians.
The freest country under heaven, with a strange
and remote Parliament possessed of power and
sometimes of will to squelch local law-making !
# * *
Nothing good or grand comes from a
colony. Melba sang as sweetly, if not so
artistically, in Melbourne for £5 as in Peters-
burg for £5OO. However, she was “colonial,”
and colonists demand a foreign trademark. To
obtain an Australian bishopric it is essential to
master theology—a facetious person once called
it the “ Queen of Sciences ” by the Isis or the
Cam. The knowledge that one is three and
three one could not he property assimilated by
the Parramatta River or the Yarra. The
author or the journalist who has something to
tell must tell it “ at home ” or be ignored. Poor
Marcus Clarke discovered that years ago,
since which many have found out the same.
Our daily and weekly journals, even those
whose incomes would support many a so-called
royal house, are costive with foreign drivel
which no Sydney or Melbourne third-class
litterateur would acknowledge by appending his
proper name. Such maundering possesses the
treble advantage of being foreign, foolish, and
cheap. Our young surgeons and physicians
spend five years in obtaining a degree which
can be secured in from three and a-half to four
in Great Britain. Even then, the Scottish and
Irish M. 8., as well as the British and colonial
public, regard the “home” professional title
with confidence and the other with either
indifference or suspicion. The atomic theory,
the properties of triangles or the peculiarities
of hexameters must be taught by professors
who come from afar, never by indigenous
scientists or scholars. Even the ladies em-
ployed to superintend the highest female educa-
tion must be imported. Why young Australian
men or women should desire to secure local
degrees in arts, physic, or science is remarkable.
* * *
Hat-bands are smuggled, beer-labels counter-
feited, trade-marks impudently appropriated,
and innumerable shabby mercantile swindles per-
petrated for vulgar pelf. It will always be so till
society is placed on an entirely different basis —
on a footing which will put a full stop to the
dreariest of human epochs—the epoch of merci-
less competition. This, however, is to be said
—the accused Melbourne traders are not with-
out excuse. They are thieves, did you say ?
Perhaps so. But what is any trading nation
when it comes to that? In the pathway of these
Victorian manufacturers opportunities were
thrown enough to turn an ordinary tradesman’s
head. Why attempt to cloak the fact that,
whenever men buy and sell, falsehood is not far
off? So far as fictitious brands are concerned
their double-dealing or double-shuffling was
the direct result of their misfortune in
belonging to a colony, the section of Avhose
inhabitants that drank lager beer and wore
plug hats being bent on obtaining both articles
from abroad. Even in the East a prophet in
his own land is nothing. In Australia he is
less. Then avoid being an Australian prophet,
at least till the provinces are firmly welded, not
under the crown. What good ever came out
of a colony ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Applause in Church.
Piucked by recent Melbourne events, the
Sydney D.T. proceeds to discuss the question :
“Should preachers be applauded?” and con-
cludes that—
It might be to the gain of both preacher and listeners
if they were placed more completely en rapport with each
ofl'er through the medium of some expression of feeling
uy the congregations.
Just so. But if “applauded,” why not “biased?”
H a preacher should lie cheered when be utters
a popular sentiment from the pulpit, it follows
that ho may he boohoood, or “ that’s-a-lied,
when he outrages the logic or emotions of his
congregation. Any sane man caught hissing in
church at the preacher gets, under the present
Australian laws, from one to five years in gaol
for sacrilege or disturbing the service. One of
the constant taunts levelled by secularists
at the clergy is, that the latter can say
what they please from the pulpit, because
the auditor has no right to dispute with
them, or comment then and there upon their
rostrum utterances, but must remain silent,
even though his whole mind be in desperate
revolt at the privileged speaker’s outpourings.
Whereas, the secularists keep an open platform
and invite anyone who chooses to do so to stand
up and contend against the views expressed.
The clergy, ever greedy of applause but in-
tolerant of criticism, have in this matter not
even a cork leg to stand upon. The parsons are
as closely protected as the lawyers. Actors, to
whom analogical reference is stupidly made by
the Telegraph, are not. There may be a dis-
turbance caused in the auditorium of a theatre,
by discontented critics, but the penal results
are fine and damages, not gaol, a close shave,
and hard labour. No actor ever seeks to con-
strue disorder into sacrilege. On the whole,
the clergy had better endure antique custom.
If they were prepared to be unanimous and
to accept all contingencies, there arc sons
of Belial enough in Parliament to pass an act
for the purpose of gratifying their desire for the
savoury salt of applause—and the stimulating
pepper of open dissent.

Not that Henry Lucy could write, but that
the Sydney Morning Herald could publish
after its own slobber the following
Voltairean analysis of the defunct Clarence,
is a marvel:
Considering he was in his 28th year his personality was
curiously unfamiliar to the populace. Of a retiring, even
phlegmatic, disposition, he shrank from all display or
participation in public affairs, and was most happy when
left alone in the company of his mother and sisters, to
whom he was devotedly attached. He had no ambition
and very few interests. In conversation he had nothing
to say, and, although naturally courteous and really
good-natured, he never exerted himself to shine in
society, however select might be the circle. When seated
at dinner he gallantly made efforts to please the neigh-
bour on right or left, venturing on remarks about the
weather, on the sport of the day, on the function he
might have participated in, or on the temperature
of the room. But the stream of small-talk speedily
ran dry, and the Prince, having done his duty, and
shown that at least he meant well, was content to
sit staving straight before him, silent and engrossed
in profound thought, probably akin to that which
baffled his royal ancestor when suddenly confronted with
the problem of how the apple got into the dumpling. He
was not artistic in his tastes as some of the Royal Family
are, nor musical as most are, though he would sometimes
sit for half an hour and jingle simple tunes on the piano.
the university he made little progress, though the
failure was not due to lack of assiduity, lie toiled ter-
ribly at first, just as he did in opening conversation at
the'dinner-table. But finding the result equally hopeless
he gave it up. He would have made a model constitu-
tional king, and had he come to the throne would have
prolonged, certainly for the period of his life, that change
in the government of the British Empire which some
people regard as inevitable.
After this, we shall expect the Fairfaxes to
recommend the elevation to the throne of a
wax figure with a phonograph in its stomach
and eyes that roll and neck that bends when a
wire is pulled, “ t Tingle simple tunes on the
piano /”■—a king ! ! !
The Melbourne lager-beer case calls attention
to an anomaly in the Victorian honorary law-
giving regulations. “No publican,” says the
Crown Law department’s secretary, “is know-
ingly allowed to remain on the magisterial roll,
neither is any tradesman of the kind considered
eligible for the bench.” On the other hand, a
publican may be elected mayor, and as mayor
he is a “beak” for two years, after the
date of his election. Consequently a succession
of hotel-keeping mayors entails the constant
presence of two licensed victuallers upon the
bench, anyway. And as the Crown Law depart-
ment never “ knows ” when a J.P. has bought
an interest in a pub. there is nothing to prevent
the judgment seat being loaded up with liquor-
sellers, whilst that proverbial h’ass, the Law, is
smugly protesting that they can’t possibly get
there. Evidently the first thing required is a
clause annulling the claim of a mayor to magis-
tracy in the event of his holding any connection
with the grog trade. Furthermore, seeing that
the unholy bias of publicans is recognised by
the statutes, the pious bigotry of total abstainers
should also be regarded as a disqualification for
the Bench. Sauce for the alcoholic goose is
sauce for the Rechabite gander.
The circumstances under which Jimmy Munro
goes “ ’ome ” to represent the honour and glory
of Victoria are unexampled in the history of
civilised Government. A public meeting, per-
haps the most thoroughly representative and
spontaneous ever held in Melbourne Town
Hall, condemned the appointment with one
voice. A petition, proving that said appoint-
ment might be cancelled, and signed by forty-
nine out of every fifty men who were asked to
write their names, was passed in to that irre-
sponsible nonentity the Governor. At a lead-
ing theatre (the Royal) the leading character
sings every night a topical verse to the effect
that the Agent-Generalship ought not to be the
last resource of a “stone-broker.” And in
every city office, warehouse, shop, factory, or
pub., the declaration that Munro is going
away in the interests of his creditors, is
honestly made and sincerely believed. Even
presuming that rumour is unfairly rough upon
the new Agent-General, the mere fact that his
first business in London wall be the winding up
of an insolvent bank must bring himself and his
billet into disrepute. Moreover, it will tell
heavily in the money - market against the
country (or kawliny) wdiich first advertises»jthe
rotten condition of its “representative”
government, and then feebly accepts the situa-
tion as inevitable. Munro may need to escape
to Hingland by the back-door if he wishes to
avoid a huge hostile demonstration, but the
thick-skinned old Scotchman will get there all
the same.

Premier Shiels’ difficulty in obtaining Minis-
terial colleagues in the Legislative Council of
Victoria, consists solely in the malicious oppo-
sition of members of that to the demo-
cratic principle of One-man-mve-vote. Shiels
wanted Agar Wynne, but as that otherwise
tolerably useful man is an out-and-outer against
the single franchise, the negotiations fell
through. Zeal, who might have accepted a
portfolio, is at present in too delicate health.
If a similar difficulty confronted a New South
Wales Premier, the latter could simply choose
outsiders and nominate them into the obstinate
Chamber ; but the Victorian Council is an
elective one. The position shows that either
form of Legislative Council is a nuisance, a
wart on democracy, and a useless collection of
propertied and tottering knackees.

Sydney D.T. is see-sawing on the question of
the railway policy of New South Wales, the
foundation-stone of which is. that all New South
Wales railways shall be owned by tire State.
It heckles the Legislative Assembly, and
especially rages at the Labor-Party, for oppos-
ing the Simpson’s Railway Bill, and then pro-
ceeds to run its log :
But in the circumstances of financial stringency in
which the colony is at present placed, considerations of
mere policy ought not to be permitted to block the way
to all such enterprises as are not undertaken by the
Stale.
“ Mere policy !” Sniff, sniff. The D.T., never
having.had any “policy,” thinks principle
quite a minor item. The tariff is a question of
“ mere policy,” yet the D.T. writhed in caco-
phonous anguish when the tariff was amended.
“ Freedom of contract” is “ mere policy,” yet
unionist opposition to it drives the D.T. into a
straight-waistcoat and a padded cell.
It (the Simpson’s Railway) would have been worked
under an Act of Parliament securing the rights and
privileges of the people— (perhaps)— and providing also
for its purchase by the State when circumstances justified
its acquisition as a public property.
The old, old game—“ Axes to grind.” First
give special privileges and concessions to
“ private enterprise ” and enable it to establish
a local monopoly, and then buy back at a fancy
price those very privileges which were the
buyer’s own free gifts. It is like giving a man
the. free use of your stable and horseflesh for a
number of years, and then paying him a heavy
sum not to use them any more. G’long !

The bankruptcy proceedings in the case of Mr
T. Smith Richardson, the gentleman who was
the sole embodiment of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, are interesting reading. Mr. Richard-
son was accustomed to deposit “ sworn state-
ments ” of the assets and liabilities of his
institution with the Government after the
manner of legitimate banks, and these sworn
statements looked well and gave pleasure to all,
and Avere a great solace to his creditors —simply
because they were “sworn.” Then, in a rash
moment, he called in an auditor, and that per-
son pointed out, among many other things, that
when a property Avas valued in the books
at £10,500, it Avas, to say the least, a
rash proceeding to add on a sum of £20,042
for interest, &c., and call the A T aluc of the
land—which, in all probability, Avas falling
rather than rising—£31,000, for no visible
reason save that Mr. Richardson felt that
Avay. Apparently Avhenever the “bank’’was
in a tight place, the “banker” simply added
on another five or ten thousand to this un-
fortunate estate, or to any other estate, or to
all of them combined, and by this process his
institution could always be made to appear
solvent in the “sworn statement,” though it
might owe £lOO,OOO and possess no assets save
the land adhering to Mr. Richardson’s boots.
The auditor, in short, declared that the institu-
tion Avas utterly insolvent, and that the ‘ ‘ sworn
statement ” was hopelessly bogus. Then the en-
raged Richardson got up on his ear, and sent
back a memorable essay as to the duties of
the humble auditor. He pointed out thathis busi-
ness Avas simply to answer the question: —‘ ‘ Docs
the balance in my books show that I owe
£74,185 3s. 2d. to depositors and £31,111 10s.
on bills in circulation as an average indebted-
ness for the quarter in session? and, secondly,
are the assets in my books as stated in the
Government Gazette?” Also he proceeded
to sib heavily on the auditor for pointing out
that, though the balance in the books showed all
these things, that balance was utterly bogus and
sham, and he stated that the auditor had ex-
ceeded his functions, and that the corres-
pondence between them had better cease. The
wild surprise of the Sydney Deposit Bank man,
that an auditor should venture to tell him his
books were unreliable, and his balance-sheet a
deception, and his institution an utter ruin, is
another proof that the average auditor doesn’t
mention these things, bub simply certifies tp the
accounts through a telescope in dead silence,
and goes away. How Mr. Richardson chanced
to strike a live auditor Avhen there are so many
dead ones about remains a mystery.

Governor Onsloav, of Maoriland, just before
he quitted the scene of his toils and his salary,
flatly refused the demand of the Ballance
Cabinet that a batch of fifteen nominees should
be sent to the Upper House in order to make
that asylum for political dead-beats a Avorkable
institution. The people of Maoriland have
elected for years past a series of the most demo-
cratic Assemblies that ever sat in any Austra-
lasian proA T ince. The democratic movement is
no sudden burst of popular frenzy, bub the
deliberate expression of public opinion, con-
firmed over and over again by the great
majority of the nation. The Assembly has
passed a large number of urgent reforms, and
the irresponsible oligarchs of the Council have
rejected them all with the calm regularity of a
machine which throws out a bill every time a
penny is dropped in the slot. Therefore, either
the system of representative government must
vanish altogether out of sight and the 50 or 60
gouty, battered, irremoveablc money-grubbers
of the Council be allowed to constitute the
nation ; or else the Upper House must be
reformed—which it can’t be unless it passes
the measure for its oavh reformation ; or it must
be abolished—which is also impossible unless it
formally enacts its oavh abolition ; or, last of all,
it must be swamped Avith a fresh batch of demo-
cratic members. It is hopeless to expect that
the Council avi 11 reform itself, and still more
hopeless to suppose that it will abolish itself,
and as Governor Onslow has just expressed his
polite regrets that he doesn’t “ see his Avay ” to
appointing fifteen new members, and has there-
upon gathered up his coronet and his carpet-
bag and the cheque for his back-salary and lied
from the scene, the Council is left master of tho
situation. «

Mr. Maurice Hervey, Principal of Illawarra
College (N.S.W.), has been devoting himself to
the Imperial Federation question, and has just
issued a volume as to its required trade policy.
The author commences by waving the Free-
trade standard and bewailing Protection, yet
proposes in the next breath to protect British
trade by the establishment of a Britannic Com-
mercial Union confined to communities owning
allegiance to the British Crown. Thus far he
has “those silken bonds” and the colonies
mainly in his eye, but he proceeds to dogmatise
thus :
The just principle should prevail not to exclude
foreigners simply because they are foreigners, but to
admit them it they choose to enter upon terms of
reciprocity.
How does Mr. Hervey propose to carry out a
reciprocal programme on Freetrade lines? His
scheme is one simply of Protection to British
commerce against the world, with the modifica-
tion of special differential duties under “most
favoured nations ” clauses to certain countries
who “reciprocate” with equal recognitions.
Foreign-trade arguments are always either pic-’
bald, or skewbald, or motley.
A Protest.
Dear Bulletin, —In the January number of the
Review of Reviews, Mr. Stead has done me the
honour to expatiate on a dialogueof mine, entitled
“ A New Capitalist,” in the current issue of the
Contemporary Review. I cannot quarrel with
Mr. Stead’s explanation of what I mean by my
dialogue (he says I “ preach my favourite doctrine
of the necessity of cultivating intelligence, at all
costs and at all hazards, as the first tiling need-
ful ”) —I cannot quarrel with this, because, of
course, ho knows very much more about what 1
mean than I do myself. That is part of his
general vocation of infallibility. He concludes
his criticism, however, by the following quotation,
which lie gives under the heading of “ Mr. Adams
says ”: —
Labour shows us in Australia, where it is alone yet
powerful enough to have anything like a free hand —•
what it is really after—and the civilisation which it rules
will be a hell of mediocrity, pullulating into corruption
and decadence ; at best a China, at worst an easy prey for
the first incursion of a more vigorous stock. It will not
advance us one step towards the true civilisation, not to,
say, towards the resolution of the great human problem.
Already the labour men decree that none but a labour
man shall stand by them. Do you guess what that
means? It means that the masses are to “run" talent
and genius to-morrow, just as the classes “ run " them
to-day, for the profit and pleasure of the “ runners ; ’’ and
once more the weary, heart-sick web shall be spun by the
stupid spider, and Nature shall sit, savage and saidonic,
enthroned on our bones, and drinking our blood from her
cups of gold, while Time, in the grey depths of space,
waits in his lethargic stupor till she, too, falls prone in an
everlasting oblivion.
When I point out that the above remarks, given
unconditionally as mine, are made by a capitalise
in keen and antagonistic discussion with a
Socialist, I fancy I am giving a sample of as
unfair a piece of alleged personal “criticism ” as
anyone could desire. The ascription of these
remarks to me, torn from all context and without
a word of explanation, seems to me to be simply a
bad case of sheer misrepresentation. I have in
Australia a few friends to whom the belief that I
had deliberately written in this way would give
pain. Others there are to whom such a shocking
right-about-face on my utterances of years would
be a stumbling-block and cause of offence. I
value all of these too highly to allow this gross
misrepresentation of me to come among them
without a strong protest. Thanking you in
advance for your kindness in allowing it to be
made in your columns, 1 am, truly yours, Francis
Adams. London, Jan. 15, 1892.

At one thing at lasto ye bould Englishmin shine,

Of the “grate wrath to come” while he bates the big

846 Maequarie-street North, Sydney, March 2, 1892,
My Dear Moorabinua, —
“ Rigoletto,” at Sydney Royal, was a “musical feast”
—Cuttica as Hilda singing like syrup, though she only
got one little knot of verdure for “ caro nome.” There
are many wicked dukes in the opera, and they are vested
(like Dante’s Beatrice) in colours of living flame; were
it not for them (and the Jester’s dress of amber
and scarlet satin) the stage would lack colour as
much as a Quaker’s seance. Because when the prima
donna is booked for persecution she never wears bright
tints, not even in her joyous youth—the shadow of the
fourth act darkles o’er her wardrobe all along, and her
clothes are as neutral-tinted as those of the uppish
servant-gal who thinks fawns and drabs make a lady.
Gilda wears two garments and a pair of breeks revealing
two supports that a wadded ballet-girl would give
her ears for, by' the way. On her first entrance she wore
a Margueritish gown of cream slashed with dull green ; in
the third, white chiffon polonaise with cascades of white
lace—and then the aforementioned hosen (which is Ger-
man for them, you know) and the top boots so that the
Jester could for evermore boast that she died in ’em, I
suppose. Signor Cuttica’s wicked duke was well-tailored
—but in the first act where he perched in his chair of
state in a gas-green coat with his mouth open he looked
for all the world like a frog catching flies for his tea.
There was a large Potts Point audience on the first
night, with one or two derelict operatic artists here and
there, who have begun to crack in their show shrieks,
glaring down Cuttica’s throat with telescopes, and look-
ing as who would say “Ah ! when I gave it—but really
she manages very well.” By the way, the d.c. (society
must mash something when Italian teams come along)
has fallen as one victim to the charms of N. Maffezzoli,
who leads the music. One fatuous female near me sighed
hoarsely, and sentimentally remarked out loud that “ she
wanted to be in the stalls so as to be near him ’’—but her
“old man” was wise enough to pin her high and dry in
the circle. As the weather threatened, dressing wasn’t
smart; the feature of the circle was the young and
cockatoo-looking bride of an octogenarian with a head as
bald as an egg—who appeared in crushed strawberry
fringed round with snowy wool. The lady looked happy.
By the way, her marriage settlement ran into five
figures.
As “Faust” took its preliminary at the Royal on a wet
and windy evening, and with the seamy side of the Opera
Co. largely on view, the circle was neither clothed nor in
its right mind, so to speak, and if it hadn’t been for Carrie
Swain’s diamonds and satin, there would have been a dead
level of dressing that wouldn’t cockle if the owner got
into a puddle or a shower of rain on the home-beat. And
a “ useful stuff” is like a useful woman—notmuch to look
at as a general thing. One gold-washed lady in the front
row had a black gown and a pair of black lace sleeves that
made her arms present a bold tattoo, and another sug-
gestive spectacle was a man in a gorgeous smoking-cap
and a trail of fusohias in his coat. Poor thing I Perhaps
he was “ engaged,” and the fancy work and floral horrors
of the half-way stage to nuptial bliss had just begun to
wreck his personal appearance in the old, old way.
“ Faust,” with its swe£t, familiar music, presents many
strange sights that I always mutely wondered at. One is
the distinct economy of Siebel’s bouquet. The Siebel-up-
to-date gets his vegetables from a florist, but the operatic
youth picks a floral offering from the girl’s garden and
lays the results of her own watering-pot upon her window,
sill just as a guarantee of good faith, I suppose. It is
never any wonder to me that Marguerite took the jewels
in preference to the dahlias and things she grew herself.
And why didn’t they tailor old Faust so that he hasn’t to
grab his gaberdine with both hands? And even then the ,
blue silk legs of Faust, junior, twinkle through a crack
looking as if the metamorphosis were starting at his feet.
Guidotti’s Marguerite wore the conventional white robe
of purity with saddle-coloured slashings and her hair in
bell-ropes tied with blue in the second and third acts. In
the fourth act a mulberry gown with black about it—
wronged virtue is as peculiar about its dressing as madame
in the Divorce-Court—and in the prison-scene white re-
lieved with straws in the hair. The warmest bit of colour
in the whole yell was Lucifer’s hot-poker suit. There is a
general impression that the Devil goes his rounds like a
roaring lion; in operatic circles, he looks more like a
roaring tomato.
I am told that the deceased semi-millionaire divided up
his big fortune very fairly, and left his bride the same
share as she would have got had she been his daughter.
She might have been his grand-daughter for that matter.
Women seem to have very foggy notions of some
things, especially when they exchange confidences in a
tram. The other day two of them were discussing a
democratic M.P.—“Oh, I can’t bear him,” said one,
“ he’s a Republican.” “What really is a Republican ?’
queried No. 2. “ Oh, a horrid man who won’t wear a tall
hat and doesn’t believe in God.” And yet some of them
want to vote. lam afraid that my sex pictures the Deity
in a belltopper.
It is always cherche\ la femme. In the “ usual divorce
case,” unravelled by Judge Windeyer recently, petitioner
said that she heard respondent was a married man and
taxed him with it. He denied it, and, moreover, said that
if she could give him the name of her informant he would
prosecute her." The wife, you see, never mentioned the
sex of her gossip, but her morganatic husband dropped
on to a woman first guess. Further down in the “un-
usual case ” petitioner confessed that her “ sister-in-law ’»
conclusively proved that the respondent was already
married. Of course her brother-in-law knew it too—but
catch a man throwing bombs into a happy home that
way. Man sits down and waits for fate to run down the
other fellow, saying that it’s none of his business ; lovely
woman lugs Nemesis along by the hair of the head.
The second of Meredith’s Ballad Concerts was held in
Sydney Town Hall, the other evening, with Baber,
Lynne, and Mrs, Hugh Duff for oantatrices, and Turner,
Rainsford, Marshall, and Farley, on the side of the
dress-suits. These “pop.” concerts (the attendance was
poor) were started on the heels of the performances of
Nicholson’s troupe from Melbourne, but it must be
remembered that the other team hadn’t cheap good
Italian opera to compete with. Lynne’s “ Ora pro nobis,”
and Turner’s shrieks about the “ Sailor’s Grave,” were the
features of the evening.
A number of musical folks dropped in to hear Mons.
Wiegand play a “Fantasia in D minor” (composed by a
Sydneyite, Mr. E. Trueman) on the Centennial organ,
last week. The budding music-constructor is studying at
Leipsic—let us hope not to eventually become only one
more unpatronised brass-plate in Paling’s top garret,
which is what they all seem to level down (or up ?) to in
this soulless land. Mr. Trueman’s father is organist at
the Anglican Church, Hunter’s Hill.
A recent excitement among churchy folks in an up-the-
line Sydney suburb was when an elder or a deacon or
somebody like that had a very small boy reprimanded
before a full congregation, and made to abjectly apologise
for writing a “personality” in the elder’s hymn-book.
The moral police in some churches are like the minions
of the Law, who generally arrest the youngest offender—
if we are to put any' faith in the harlequinades of a million
'mimes.
That is an odd story about a sporting professional man
down South (since retired) whose wife went to America
with her child, whither hubby promised to follow her
sooner or later. But a lady with a fine figure on horse-
back won the Freetrade affections of this promising
grass-widower in the interim, and while his wife was
quietly waiting for him in New York he espoused
the new idea and —l2 months later impudently
and recklessly' sent a copy of his “ marriage ” certificate
and that of the birth of a fine olive-branch to the woman
who was waiting for him “ down under.” It is just
possible that his wife—though a sensitive woman who
would (ordinarily') rather die than figure in a police-court
on a maintenance hunt—will not be able to meekly stand
that insult, and, after all, there might be some nice pick-
ings for the lawyers and the evening press.
Sydney Women’s Literary Club (that is the way it ought
to be put) is supposed to fall down and worship at the
shrine of Johnsonian English, and yet a member—of the
committee, too ! —was talking at the top of her voice the
other day about somebody or something being “horrid.’’
Horrid is schoolgirl, not dictionary, English—quite as
dreadful a word as “ awfully’,” for using which any member
is liable to be stood in the corner for a week. The com-
mittee at least should talk like a book if it wants to
encourage its disciples.
Mr. and Mrs. Woodin entertained a crowd of people at
their residence, “ Cassandra,” Bondi, on 24th ult. Floor
and music were as nice as could be, and the decora-
tions of pied art silk and elf-lights were most artistic and
original. Mrs. Woodin wore a trained dress of cream
brocade, with gold bullion trimming and cream chiffon
sleeves, diamonds on the corsage and in the hair, and her
two juvenile daughters were in white silk and pink silk
respectively. Most of the lady guests wore virgin white,
and the room resembled a snow-storm sometimes in
consequence.
Sydney University commemorates on 9th April this
year, and as it will thus fall on a Saturday—mob day—
the Dons ought to hold it in the Domain, so that the
audience might have a chance to climb a tree to see what
really is going on. Governor Jersey will doubtless go in
fear and trembling. Last time a rude undergrad, actually
requested him to “ shut up !”
When a mail-steamer homeward bound hauled off from
Circular Quay the other day, a married couple in the
saloon began rushing to and fro in an excited manner
asking that the engines should be backed because their
baby and nurse-girl were left behind. The blessed infant
on the wharf was still visible to the naked eye, and as its
shrill howl wafted across the water some of the passengers
thought of asking the captain to leave it where it was in
the interests of peace and quiet. But the gallant tar
stopped the vessel in mid-stream—a tender steamed up
with the precious child on deck, and the voyage was
resumed to a chorus from all the single women on board
of “ an’ did Poppsie Woppsie fink its mumma and puppa
had left it on ze naughty wharf zen?"
Yours affectionately,

In N.S. W. Assembly last week, Mr. G. D.
Clark rose to complain that the hangman had, on
the ground that “ he was a civil servant ” (which
he is, with a salary of .£180), refused to look after
a certain flower-bed at Darlinghurst Gaol. The
Bulletin wants to know why any hangman
should be required to look after flowers ? Is there
anything appropriate in the spectacle of a noseless
executioner touching up the lily, and smelling the
heliotrope, and gently tending the bright red rose
with the same hairy hand which adjusts the noose
under the ear of the condemned ? We know an
Australian gaol-governor who possessed a garden
of which he was very proud, and in it an aloe of
which he was proudest of all. But one day the
flogger came down there to officially whale a
prisoner, and as he passed he snuffed at the aloe,
and straightway that unfortunate plant withered
up and died. If the hangman is to look after a
flower-bed, we hereby suggest, with this catas-
trophe in view, that he should do it with his head
disguised in a kerosene-tin.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “ Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
To the inventive genius of a lady in 1843
are we indebted for the first ice-cream freezer,
but those ladies who wish to save time, labour,
and money invest in the new German Freezer.
Why ? There is no handle to turn, not half so
much ice used, and it keeps the cream firm in or
out doors. Two quarts of ice cream can be made
for Is. 6d. to Is. 9d. Send for Sydney testimonials
to. T. A. Knight & Co., St. James Chambers,
King-street, Sydney, who supply the machine.
Price, 305., with instructions so simple that a
child can understand.
The Chow’s Friend.
The Rev. F. Hastings, of Adelaide, speaking at
a meeting held in that city of many churches,
last week, said that “ he would like to see a great
influx of Chinese to Australia.”
The Reverend Hastings, F.,
(Good Lord preserve us all ?)
He is a perfect Meph-
Istopneles—in small.
For, sure, it must be scorn
Of whites—or a disease—
Makes him exalt his horn
In praise of the Chinese.
He’d have them here, he would,
These Pagans clean and chaste,
lie finds them very good—
We don’t admire his taste.
And yet it may be fine—
His taste, why should we flout?
A hunger quite divine
He has, without a doubt.
For saving from the coals
Of—where the sinner fries
Forever—precious souls
That squint through slanting eyes.
His fancy seems outre
To us ; he doesn’t care
For trifles such as, say,
A leper here and there.
This pearl of clergymen
With courage to the brim
Is filled—an opium-den
No terrors has for him.
He’s with the Mongol bland
In uridisgusted touch,
He loves the coolie, and—
He coolly says as much.
Instead of Christian 100
To aid a church-bazaar,
Their heathen pak-a-pu
He would prefer by far.
’Twould be a problem rare
This taste to backward trace,
Ethnologists would swear
’Twas something in the race,
And go on to explain,
And make it clear as mud,
There’s some Mongolian strain
A-working in his blood.
One thing’s, however, clear—
And this he surely sees ?
They are not welcome here,
His meek and mild Chinese.
And if he’s really “struck ”
On them particular,
Why don’t he try his luck
In China—where they are?
If he should do so, we
Will write his epitaph,
Because, most probably,
His pets—it makes us laugh
On whom his soul doth dote,
With humour, weird and grim.
Will calmly cut his throat—
And make a saint of him.
The Great Unwashed—the Broken Hill
people.

The Institute of Journalists was set fairly
going in Melbourne last Saturday with 92 mem-
bers and the immediate prospect of many more.
Twenty-nine were nominated for the first council
of twelve, and the meeting elected five from the
Age, four from the Aegus, and one each from the
Telegeaph, Standaed, and Hansaed. William
Carrington, one of the Herald subs., was elected
hon. sec. It was decided to ask Chief Justice
Higinbotham to reconsider his refusal of the pre-
sidency, and S. V. Winter and Harry Short, the
latter of whom presided, were elected vice-presi-
dents without opposition.
Every copy of the original edition of
Mudie’s “Felonry of New South Wales” that
came to Sydney was, for some years, sent by
Sydney booksellers to a certain well-known high-
toned lady, by whom, as received, the books were
committed to the flames with a prayer that the
like fate might be even then visiting the Quilplike
author—who, by the way, “ Price Waning” holds,
was far from being as black as he was painted.
Our contributor believes that Mudie was held
responsible for many acts committed by another, in
Mudie’s interest, but without Mudie’s knowledge.
There was a good deal of the virtuoso about Mudie.
One of his hobbies—the fact is now published for
the first time—was the collection of medals ; and
there must be knocking about the country some-
where the manuscript catalogue of the results of
his own gatherings, Major-General Stewart, for
17 days Governor of the colony, was presented by
Mudie with some rare duplicate impressions. Per-
haps they are now in the Museum at the “ Mount ”
—the Stewarts’ Estate, Bathurst —the same insti-
tution where guests were shown the General’s
Waterloo sword. Which, considering he wasn’t
at the battle, was rather a curiosity,
Newcastle (N.S.W.) possesses an artistic
criminal whose love for work has caused a vast
amount of trouble. In the earlier stages of his
nefarious career he used to get into Maitland gaol
now and then for drunkenness, and on these
occasions he was employed in the dull monotony
of raatrmaking—a job which in its horrible dreari-
ness can only be compared with the eternal
“ stitch—stitch—stitch ” of the shirtmaker toiling
in her attic. But on one unfortunate occasion he
was set to work on a big job of masonry, and he
took such a fancy to this novel employment that,
when his term was up, he asked the chief warder
to keep the work for him, as he intended coming
back to finish it. Thereupon he went out, bailed
up an astonished passer-by, and wrested his
loose cash from him. He got back promptly to
his beloved gaol, and pegged away at his favourite
employment. When his second sentence was
finished he tore himself away reluctantly, and
told the chief warder that he meant to get in
again promptly for robbery or something, and take
another spell at the work. In pursuance of this
promise he went into a shop and seized the till in
the presence of the astonished proprietor. He
got three months for this, but his contract being
a very large one, even that didn’t finish it, and
therefore, being once more ejected from the
gaol, he got another short term for a violent
assault on a policeman. Also, he has done
various sentences for breaking windows, welting
harmless strangers without provocation, petty
larceny, damage to street lamps, and riot. The
masonry contract is now—thank heaven—nearly
concluded. On a rough estimate two more assaults
on the police, the breakage of a large sheet of
plate-glass, the use of bad language in a church,
and depositing rubbish in a public place, combined
with unlawfully disinterring a dead body, keeping
a common gambling-house and cutting off a
Chinaman’s pigtail will about see it through ; and
after that, if they set this offender to mat-making
again, he will probably retire from the gaol for
good.

On Monday “Faust” was represented
with an entirely new cast. Signora Cuttica
represented Margherita, her husband sang the
music of the title role, Signor Sisco upheld the
honour of Valentine, and Signor lorio sacrificed
his sensibilities to the demands of Mephistofele.
The devil of this cast was a serious one; he acted
solemnly, as though time were valuable, and he
sang with a rotund crispness suggestive of a fallen
angel. He lacked the sardonic merriment of his
predecessor, but he was evidently much inearnest,
and laughing seemed to be to him a painful and
altogether unnecessary sacrifice to the heathen
Momus. His conception of the part was, how-
ever, clean-cut and consistent. He never laughed
out of place, and he objected to sing unless
the situation required it. He was altogether
a practical and business-like piece of astral villainy,
of Presbyterian tendencies and a leaning towards
the Baptists of the Hardshell persuasion. Signor
Cuttica’s Faust, without being magnificent, was
sympathetic and artistic. Fie cannot climb up to
a high note with the coppery precision of a Vilalta,
but he gets there just the same, and you like him
the better for it. Siernor Sisco’s Valentine was
far from being as dramatically fine as Melossi’s,
but he looked like the King of Spain in disguise,
with cross-gartered legs and an interest in war-
medals. Marta and Siebel were carefully attended
to by Signorine Cavalleri and Castiglioni, and all
present on both representations were sorry that
these two genuine artistes had not a more spacious
opportunity of enlightening humanity on many
operatic points. The Margherita of the cast was
Signora Cuttica, a vocal gem of something more
than purest ray serene, whose singing and acting
cannot find a comparison on this side of the line,
and whose total presentation of Gounod’s im-
mortal heroine was absolutely flawless. Signora
Cuttica alone is worth the price of admission, and
she did much to redeem the yaucheries of the
chorus and the banalities of the Woolloo-
mooloo brass band ; she gave a charm to the
Levantine pirates who masqueraded as Nnremburg
citizens, and she assuaged the red-striped blankets
of the alleged warriors who gyrated through a
Faustine century. The army of the present pro-
duction was especially strong, numbering, as it
did, nearly sixteen and a half more or less full-
bodied supers. The dresses and scenery were also
very superb, recalling to our minds many operas,
from “Boeaccio” to “ Pepita, ” and innumerable
dramas, from William Baconshaketo Pettit Sims-
On Monday night the orchestra celebrated the

the overture to “ The Barber of Seville,” On
Thursday Verdi’s triumphant “Un Ballo m
Maschera ” was put through the operate null
with results pending future remarks.

Sydney Her Majesty’s is still peacefully
meandering towards the apotheosis of Marguerite.
George Rignold continues to hold the mirror up
to limelight, and to limp placidly with a mocking
laugh to the realms of darkness. His perform-
ance of a hollow devil with an audible leer has
been productive of much happiness to the good
folk who believe in eternal brimstone, but, un-
fortunately for the permanence of such bliss,
George Henry V. R, is himself becoming tired
of the unsubstantial mockery and yearns for some-
thing new. Miss Kate Bishop also has illustrated,
in a becoming costume, the woes of the sainted
girl whom Faust deceived, and G. P. Carey ha
been nightly drunk in an artistic manner for a
longer period than he cares for. Likewise, trie
limelight man is a wreck of his former self an
wants a holiday. H, R. Jewett is, in addition-
tired of chasing, in a blue suit, a lady who elude

Melbourne Princess’s is generally filled to
lotion notwithstanding hard times, and the
r *Mdv duke’s moss-green stockings are unfavour-
ably commented upon by all classes of society.
Tie unnatural contortions of the alleged ballet
.[‘gel wlio “runs” His Grace and diverts his
flections from their lawful course are also the
!i biect of mild protest, but with these minor
exceptions “ La Cigale ” ranks as a huge popular
‘ There are times, indeed, when the
bursts of approbation would lift the roof off, if it
lidii’t conveniently slide back and allow the stars
above to twinkle-twinkle at the bright little star
below. Marie Halton’s intelligent vocalisation
creates a furore in at least two places. It is the
palpable result of Parisian study of the original,
•uul sorely tempts one to speak boldly of the
gifted artiste as a choice French plum. But for
certain reasons this figure of speech can only be
hinted at. The Bulletin is officially informed
that it has already lacerated, inadvertently, the
feelings of Miss Halton with a Poverty Pointed
paragraph, fashioned as follows :
The dress-circle of Melbourne Princess's held quite a
Gaiety Co. crush of no-forehead Johnnies on the first
„i„ht of “ La Cigale.” “ Dooeid fine tart! V they said—
and her husband was in a stage-box, and the son is
nearly 16.
Herewith The Bulletin coughs apologetically
and rises to explain that the most potent of weekly
publications is powerless to prevent young gentle-
men of no intellect whatever from referring to the
fairest of Eve’s daughters as a pumpkin pie, if
they feel so disposed. As a matter of politeness
we call upon the browless brotherhood to with-
draw their words, but we are not prepared to
affirm that Miss Halton isn't a fine sample
of Nature’s confectionery. And seeing that
admiration for the opposite sex has been vented in
extravagant language since Eve first called Adam
“a duck of a feller ” the present difficulty might
be met by the issue of a managerial manifesto to
the aforesaid Johnnies, desiring them to worship
their newest idol in terms selected by herself and
published on the back of the bill. Personally we
are willing to call the lady anything she likes,
from a snowflake to a little faded flower,
but we must call her sum’ut or we shall
explode. For the rest of the paingiving par.
we cheerfully cancel the information that Miss
Halton’s husband was in a stage-box, not because
he wasn’t there, but because his presence in any
place is of no real theatrical import. Henceforth
he can be on the roof for all The Bulletin will
care, and if ever he should happen to fall off a
tram, or choke himself with indignation, we will
endeavour to maintain a death-like silence on the
matter. As regards the irrelevant observation
that “the son is nearly 16,”it would appear to be
a printer’s misplacement, and probably belonged
by right to another paragraph in a distant part of
the paper. Somebody else’s son was 16. If Miss
Halton has a son he must be a teeny-weeny,
flaxen-haired darling of not more than three, and
half a fairy on the mother’s side. Miss Halton,
we feel much pleasure in suggesting, is far too
young to claim the maternity of a full-grown boy,
and any future observations on her undoubted
talent must be understood to mean that she
promises to blossom into an extraordinary prima
donna eventually, when she is much older and has
gamed a va,st deal more experience of the stage.
I he penalties of youth cannot be avoided.

“The Green Lanes of England,” too)
brought a fair-sized audience to the Alexandra
last Saturday. There are enduring charms about
this intensely British drama, and its greenness
never seems to fade. The hero is drowsily drunk
in a general way, but occasionally they blow a
trumpet, and then he shakes the beer-drops from
his mane and recites “ The Charge of the Light
Brigade ” in a manner that knocks the trumpet
clean out of time. The villain is a plain, straight-
forward character who lives by the sweat of
his brow as a blackmailer. When two or
three young women stand between him and
black mail he chucks ’em in the rippling
stream and cries aloud that they are dead
—although they haven’t been in the water
three seconds and the surrounding country is
within easy reaching distance all the time. The
typical British fayther is another classic prowler
round “The Lares.” His hoary curse is familiar
in his mouth as household words. In times of
peace he tenderly chews his curse and rolls it
round his teeth till his daughter has been be-
trayed, whereupon he disgorges the dainty morsel
and totters off. A fayther who has got rid of his
curse is not much good. He reminds one of a bee
that has lost its sting, or a crocodile with its teeth
drawn, and he might just as well be dead. Dam-
pier’s performance of Gentleman George is one of
the most popular articles on his long list. Sundry
other characters are vigorously played, and Miss
Lily Dampier will doubtless profit by the celestial
advice tendered on Saturday night, when a voice
from the gods cried —“Chuck it off your chest.”
Hi Hi * *
Carl Hertz and the Graggs Family are
steadily blowing the bubble reputation for George
Musgrove’s show at Melbourne Opera House.
Hertz in himself constitutes a continual change
of programme, and his entertainment has a mag-
netic influence upon certain people. Curiosity as
regards conjuring tricks is a complaint from which
ladies and larrikins are exempt, but middle-aged
men take it badly sometimes (even as they are apt
to fall in love), and already the Opera House is
haunted by a few of these unfortunates. They
come early and often, and watch the magician
with wild, bloodshot eyes, but they never get any
nearer to the bottom of his harmless delusions.
A man once wrote to Hertz, stating that he
had attended two hundred performances of the
same trick in London, and yet lie wasn’t
happy. He wanted it to be put back on
the bill so that he could come and see it a few
more times before he died. The Melbourne press
has acquired a taste for Hertz. One night last
week the sub-editor of a solemn organ formed
himself into a committee of enquiry and went
upon the platform to improve his mind. Hertz
put him in a chair that was charged with electri-
city, or liquid dynamite, or something, and the
effect was awful. A sudden rush of ideas to the
gentleman’s brain caused him to bound from the
chair like a young gazelle, and for two days after-
wards he used to sit down upon his head and
dash off brilliant paragraphs with his right leg.
The halls, &0., are fairly in it this race week, and
the final departure of the lugubrious explorer has
filled a long-felt want.

Apropos Signorina Emelia Guidotti's approaching
marriage to restaurateur Rubina, of Melbourne— the
sooner the Signorina marries this young man the better ;

Over Hertz and the Craggs, the reigning Musgrove has
the familiar difficulty—Who is to get the first line on the
bill, or ad. ? The see-saw is so nice, and one party
appears to have complained of the apparently unavoid-
able offence of putting one first, wherefore they are side-
by-side, without even an “and,” w’hich might imply
some trifling inferiority. But there have been cases
where even this would not do, and the ingenious manager
has had to invent a criss-cross style of printing the
names, so that there could he no suspicion as to which
was first.
In Melbourne, G. V. Brooke lived in a whilom pleasant
villa which still remains, in Flinders Lane East, between
Stephen and Spring-streets. It is a one-storey brick place,
nicely verandahed, and stood in a beautiful garden, slop-
ing down to Flinders-street. Here Brooke used to study.
Delicious little parties assembled, and a Sunday afternoon
was nice. James Smith, David Blair, Tom Pavey, and a
few more survive of the cronies—not to speak of the
Artful, who is the G. V. Brooke mine. If we want any
copy all we have to do is to “’ave a glass along a’ me,’
under the Shakespeare statue on George’s Richmond
Loggia.

Dear Bulletin, — In a recent paragraph you mention
Mrs. “Jemmy” Grimes as intending to introduce a
“ oorrohoree ” set of music, and remark that this is
probably the first time such a Wagnerian feat has ever
been attempted. Not so. The opening ode at Melbourne
Exhibition of 1866 or thereabouts, called the “South Sea
Sisters”—music by C. E. Horsley, words by “Orion”
Horne—contained a “corrohoree chorus.” I was present
at a rehearsal, where the chorus signally failed to get the
hang of the corrohoree yell. Horne came forward, and,
after a few explanatory words, patterned that yell for us
in a hair-raising and blood curdling manner. This number
at the opening concert received a triple encore. Al-
together, that musical festival had some unique features.
The entire combination—orchestra, chorus, C. E. Horsley,
and all—was “bossed” by a forceful lady named Mrs.
Carl fischer. The orchestra was “ assisted ” by the third
part of an organ—the management couldn't run the cost
of a complete instrument. The chorus included Dan
Beaumont, Ingmire, Blanchard, Exon, Williams, Madden,
and many others more or less known to fame, while
Donaldson, Angus, Maggie and Bertha AVatson, Shenvin,
and the Carandinis warbled the solos. Of the orchestra,
all I remember was that it had a daisy kettle-drummer,
known as Signor Pietro Ganna, who used in off-moments
to revel in warlike inspirations, representing the battle of
Dolly's Bray, or the siege of Jerusalem, or something of
the kind.—Yours, The Ciioweater.

When Carl Hertz was in New York he played the
“vanishing cage” trick upon a peculiarly massive and
sceptical countryman, who had lumbered on to the stage
with a fixed bucolic intention of standing no nonsense.
In due course Hertz gave the cage to this stranger, with
instructions to grab it tight between the two legs of
mutton that served him as fists. There was the cage, and
the canary chirping on the perch. The countryman
hung on to his prey like grim death. Hertz fired a pistol,
or clapped hands, or blew his nose, and in an instant the
grim agriculturalist found himself clutching wildly at
nothing at all. He breathed hard and murmured “H —!”
pianissimo. But when Hertz had pulled back his shaggy
coat and produced the canary from the rural gentleman’s
armpit his feelings could no longer be restrained. He
walked slowly to the footlights and declared himself “ the
son of a &e.
