The Wild-Cat Column.
An old Ballarat digger years ago discoursed on
one of his experiences. Working for wages in a face
single-handed one night he struck two magnificent nug-
gets worth over £lOOO. Prompted by poverty and the
absence of the shift-boss, he retired to a worked-out place,
hid the treasures, and then returned to “ graft,” but,
unable to work through excitement, he fell on his knees
and prayed for Divine guidance as to the disposal of the
gold. After much earnest entreaty, something “struck
him.” It was to hand the gold to the rightful owner,
which he did by turning it over to the boss, to whom,
however, he, of course, said never a word as to the con-
flict in his mind. Well do we remember the hearty
exclamations—“ You did the right thing, William—God
answered your prayer, Bill,”—and so on, which broke in
at this part of the yarn. Better still, somehow, do we
remember William’s conclusion—“ And I’ve been kicking
myself ever since for being such a mug.”
» # •
Mount Stewart (N.S.W.) is coming into pro-
minence. It was the same with Lewis Ponds, Costigan,
Cordillera, and other silver-fields at a similar stage of
their existence. The ore-body is reported to be enormous,
the assays published from week to week are influencing
the sbaremarket, and the local correspondent talks of the
“future which Leaderville has before it.” Now, all these
things occurred in other eases where the future was a
ghastly failure. What one needs to know is merely this:
“ Do the published assays represent the bulk average of
the ore-body ?” If they don’t, they are a delusion and a
snare, and if they do, the public ought to be so informed
or they should not be published. The mining investor
ought by now to have lost his faith in assays as a guarantee
of bulk value.
• # *
The celerity with which assayers used to put
through samples in the boom was once clearly explained
to the writer: “ You see, boom times we get more samples
than half-a-dozen assayers could do, but we don’t turn
away work, and get through every sample by employing
a smart boy merely to fill up the certificates of unlikely-
looking rock. You see, it’s merely reducing the thing to
a matter of simple dictation.”
« # »
The evolutions of the notorWs “Last-Call”
mine have been extraordinary. Mark Hammond, whose
genuineness throughout his entire connection with it
nobody who knows him for one moment questions,
encountered a series of troubles before he trot the
“under-offer” properly fixed. There were so many “ in
it ” that it is said lie went away loaded with agreements.
Under the impression that the liability ended with the
few pounds put up to send a man ahead, several of the
syndicate instantly struck when asked to put in a marked
cheque for £lOOO to make up the required £lO,OOO for
purchase in the event of Hammond’s approval. He
declined to budge without it, consequently others were
found to take their place, and as one retired at the last
moment, nine represented £lO,OOO. When it was pro-
claimed one of the mineral wonders of the world, the
retiring gentlemen came forward with wonderful com-
placency, and almost fainted when informed that they
weren’t in it. Another large holder who took an early
opportunity of standing on velvet declared on selling
that if it were a frost he would return the purchasers’
money—a promise which appears to have completely
faded from his memory. The anxiety to get out on the
part of persons who by their knowledge should have been
the last, excited suspicion, but unfortunately for the
public the confidence reposed in Hammond's judgment
covered the statements made by disinterested persons
who examined the property unde' 1 entirely different
circumstances.
# * #
The Last Call’s half-yearly report without much
detail admits the mine is a fizzle. The directors regret
that the shareholders have been made the victims of
villainy, but attempt consolation by stating the fact that
they themselves ore the greatest losers by the whole
transaction. They also hold out the flickering candle of
hope by saying that inasmuch as the gold used to salt the
mine undoubtedly came originally from it, operations
will he continued witli the view of striking other rich
veins. The mining-manager, however—and ho ought to
know best—apprehends that the formation is one in
which nothing of permanent value can he found. The
reports clearly prove one thing—that this mine was not
salted in a day or by one man without the knowledge of
others ; that much more time and care were taken in pul-
ing gold into the various places than is usually exercised
in taking gold out of an ordinary mine. On the question
of what steps the directors propose to take with regard
to the suspected salters the report is discreetly silent.
Something may be done if only to satisfy shareholders—
those who bought at £2 especially—but it will end in
smoke. Experience has shown that to “pot” a salter is
harder than to “pot” the Czar.
* *
Distance lends enchantment to a gold-mine. To
make money easily out of a real “duffer,” let it he away
in the back country beyond any railroad or coaching
town ; if there is not even a telegraph w ithin a hundred
miles so much the better. Better still if it is an abso-
lutely new discovery with no old mines around. Under
these circumstances the local inhabitants, if there are
any, will by their ignorance innocently confmmany state-
ments. The man who runs a paper in the town one
hundred miles aw ay will accept everything as gospel and
declare that that town being in command of the traffic
will bulge out, get a railway, and otherwise become a
great centre owing to the discovery. The gist of the local
paper’s editorial will be flashed to Sydney, and the gist of
that will appear in the metropolitan journals, for is he
not the correspondent of all of them ? Only those who
are “ in it” will undertake the journey in the early stage
of the boom. By and by, when the burst comes, an in-
vestor will go up to see the mine. He finds that the
original owners have vanished, the employes are all
drunk at the local pub., and that the small shoot of gold
on which the boom was built has been taken right out —
probably on a shovel.
* * #
We have been asked advice concerning several
mines at Pambula. -Now, what would you say to your
friends if you saw ’em quietly sitting in a house which was
on fire ?
# * #
The true mining tipster tips himself; the false
one, depend upon it, is himself tipped.
* * *
Beware of the kerbstone mining vampires you
can fall across any day in Pitt-street, Sydney, between
Bridge and King streets. They toil not, neither do they
spin, but have hitherto fattened on the grass-like green-
ness of the ordinary N.S.W. mining investor. Their
stock-in-trade usually consists in a few' pocket-worn speci-
mens, which are the last thing thev part with, and which
have done duty for many mines.
* m m
The man, too, who represents that the presence
of water, or the absence of air in a mine, caused the ori-
ginal owners to leave treasures behind them, should be
headed off. No miner ever turned his back on a proved
golden claim since the world began.
# # *
Turn a deaf ear to the man who tries to make
you believe that the value of a mine is gauged by a trial-
crushing which he has made of a ton of ore.
* # *
Be not wheedled into a syndicate, under the
blissful idea that you are “the first robber.” We have
been there, and have found that instead of being “the
first robber,” we were the first robbed.
* # #
Nevertheless, bear well in mind that all mining
is a gamble and that as long as Nature gambles with her
earth treasures, man will gamble with human nature. To
talk of making mining merely an investment is to talk
nonsense. To make horse-racing an investment would be
comparatively easy.
* * *
Where gambling is a necessity swindles will
always be worked, and the line where, in mining, honesty
ends and dishonesty begins will never be defined. To
give quite a small nut to crack—would the man be honest
or dishonest who, finding a rich reef on another’s pro-
perty, made all arrangements for working same ere re-
vealing his discovery to the owner ?
* * #
A startling fact, A man recently travelled in a
train all the way from Adelaide to Broken Hill without
meeting one single person who disclosed the price at
which he was once offered a fourteenth share in the Pro-
prietary mine !
# • #
Next to the salter —the man who puts gold into
a mine—the mine-thief, the man who takes the gold out
of it, is difficult to gaol. To lay wait for him in a room,
to see him go into a rich stope ; to shoot him when he
came out, would only end in an action by you for trespass
or by him for injury, unless he at that moment had speci-
mens in his possession.
* * *

There is a whisper in London of civil legal
proceedings being taken by the debenture-holders
of a big Sydney investment company against the
directors in Sydney on the ground of misapplica-
tion of funds. It is alleged that the directors
have exceeded their powers by devoting to other
purposes money subscribed for the purpose of in-
vestment in freehold land. Counsel’s opinion was
being taken when the mail left.
Parkes “ wants to know”:—
“In the event of the N.S.VV. Electoral Bill becoming
law, will the usual course be adopted and Parliament
dissolved to enable the electors to exercise their privileges
n electing a new Assembly under the reformed law ?”
The Bulletin devoutly trusts it will, notwith-
standing the fact that when Parkes’ Electoral Bill
seemed in a fair way to become law he showed no
sign of dissolving Parliament “to enable the
electors to exercise their privileges,” &c. But the
old man is in too great a hurry to grab for the
money-bag. Even with the best of good fortune,
by the time the Electoral Bill is passed and
the Seats Redistribution Bill has been crowded
through, the present Parliament will probably
have been something very like 18 months in exist-
ence. Then tire x-olls for all New South Wales
will have to be prepared entirely afresh, and by the
time that work is finished, the Assembly will be in
a fair way to expire of old age. The chief danger
is, not that the dissolution will be postponed too
long, but that by some unforeseen accident it may
come too soon, before the new rolls are ready, and
that the Foreign-trade-plural-voting crowd may
thus have one more chance with their nefarious
policy under the tail-end of the old system.

Once more the scheme is revived for the
Pacific cable, to unite Australia with America,
but “loyalty” tries to quash it. Twenty years
ago the surveys, by the U.S. frigate Tusca-
rora, proved that the ocean floor is most facile for
this cable. Surely it is all important for us to be
in daily communication with the Great Republic,
far more so than with England.

The title “Commonwealth of Australia”
puzzled the editors of the Almanack de Gotha
for 1892, when treating of the Sydney Convention
of last year. They could not translate it save as
the “ Republique de I’Australie ” or something of
that sort, which would be an official rending of
the silken cords that would never pass muster.
So they put the English phrase in untranslated
and vary it by calling us the “ Etats-Unis de
I’Australie.”
The goldmine of the monopolist is Free
Competition—Freetrade in Labour. Overcrowded
England is being injected with continuous doses
of cheap-labour Poles who are squeezing out the
local bootmakers by labouring 100 hours weekly
for from Gs. to Bs. In Bananaland the white mail
is being thrust aside by imported Kanakas and
Italians and Javanese and Malays. In West
Australia the eager-mawed capitalist is howling
for Chinese ! —and lots of them.
One Manger, who died in Melbourne last
week, played a rather important part, as a Graham
Berry supporter, in the Geelong elections afore-
time. This was when Garratt carried on his
untiring persecution, as some termed it, of Berry.
Garratt also fought Berry at the elections, and
the grave charges he made against the present
knight resulted in the historical Parliamentary
Committee. Garratt’s next game was to move
heaven and earth to get the evidence printed, but,
by some inexplicable influence, he was foiled, or
the history of Victoria would have been changed.
When Lamartine was head of the Provi-
sional Government, in ’4B, the crowd in front of
the Hotel de Ville thrust forward the red flag as
the standard of the Second Republic. Lamartine
rejected it, saying, “The red flag has only been
round the Champ de Mars—the tricolour has
been round the world.” No one could say so
to-day. The red flag has gone right round the
world, from Paris to Melbourne, and from London
to Odessa. It floats over no fortress, and flies
at no masthead, and is an object of hatred to
every capitalistic government in the world. But
the people are everywhere learning to look to it
as their flag—the flag of the workers as opposed
to the cut-throat flags which mean war and class
despotism. It is the Only really international flag
on earth.

forms which surrounded them in the street and in
the house—the forms of the freest people the
world has ever known. Edward Gibbon Wake-
field wrote that Australia would probably produce
a race as perfect physically as the Greek type,
“ but they must be free men.” Just so; as free
from the paralysing influences of caste and
privilege as from the physical tyranny expressed
in the soldiers’ spiked helmet; as free from the
squalid misery induced by British competitive in-
dustrialism as from the sordid viciousness of the

In Turkey no man’s house can be seized
for debt. When an agriculturist is sold up, his
creditors must leave his home and a certain area
of land, sufficient to support him and his family,
untouched, and no legal process whatever can
deprive him of this property. If a man possesses
an estate but no house, and the Ottoman bill-of-
sale person swoops down upon him, he must not
only leave his debtor sufficient ground to furnish
him with sustenance, but must build upon it a
house according to certain fixed and unalterable
plans and specifications, ere he is allowed to sell
the rest of the property. The device whereby the
bloated Christian throws his bankrupt debtor out
into the snow is unknown among the gentle
Moslems, despite the efforts of British missionaries
to_ teach them the great blessings of rum and
eviction.
“ There is no country in the world where
the number of Civil Servants is so large in pro-
portion to the total population as in N.S. Wales,”
said Rose, M.L.A., while booming the cause of
alleged retrenchment in the cornstalk Assembly.
That is just where the Argyll economist shows
his astonishing ignorance. There is no country in
the world where there are so many legislators—
most of them stupid ones—in proportion to popu-
lation as in N.S. Wales; but the Civil Service, as
the term is correctly understood, is smaller in
that much-abused country than in any other we
know of. The cornstalk “reformer” always com-
pares the N.S.W. service with that of England,
and howls at the discrepancy between the two, and
he doesn’t even know that in England, policemen,
school teachers, railway employes, and the like—
who form the great bulk of the official classes in
Australia—are not included at all. Why doesn’t
the N.S.W. Skinflint-party start by reducing the
Legislative Assembly—the thing above all others
which urgently wants cutting in half ?

depositors, and there ate in France 7,000,000
owners of property above one acre in size, as
against 300,000 in England. “In England the
average extent of a single farm is 390 acres; in
France 10 acres (4,000,000 owners, holding pro-
perties of two acres); while farms of 200 acres are
so few that they can be counted on the fingers.
In France there are 8,000,000 acres of common
land, the exact amount which has in England

“Joe Brown is dead.” “What did he
die of?” “He died of a Tuesday.”
Perth (VV.A.) Daily Hews is almost
entirely typo’d by women, and, of course, at the
lower female scale of payments. If fully com .
petent female compositors were admitted into
typographical unions they could and must of
necessity insist on union rates of payment. Tho
other alternative is for the women to form their
own unions, and fix their scales on the same
standards as do the men. How would that result
ye prophets? A fairy blend? Or a stand-up
fight ? Or amalgamation and a big stride toward
evolving the women’s-rights problem ?
HE NEVER FELT ASHAMED.
The alligator heeds no prick,
It does not make him cross ;
’Tis hard to tickle \yitli a stick
An old rhinoceros:
The elephant shows ne’er a weal
When bricks at him are aimed—
And Jimmy has no cause to feel
A little bit ashamed.
The hide of brither James Munro
Is tough and bullet-proof,
His skull is galvanised, as though
It were an iron roof.
Profuse abuse affects him not,
He cares not what he’s named,
But snaps his fingers at the let
And doesn’t feel ashamed.
He deemed that “meeting to protest”
A Caledonian joke,
lie chose the berth that seem-ed best
For one so nearly “broke”;
It offered him a resting-place,
And, being sorely lamed,
He took it after saying “ Grace ”
And didn’t feel ashamed.
Though vulgar sinners scoff and frown
In virtue he is strong—
The man who represents the Crown
Can never do a wrong.
And why should Jimmy care a straw
How often he is blamed ?
The country cannot pass a law
To make him feel ashamed.
To greatness certain folks are born,
To some it comes by luck,
Whilst greatness from the nettle scorn
’Tis possible to pluck.
Through life and also when he’s dead
For this shall Jim be fatned—
Though curses rained upon his head
He never felt ashamed.
When Doctors Disagree!
Professional etiquette almost always prevents
one doctor finding fault with another’s” treatment
no matter how erroneous he may consider it, but
a case where they quite differed occurred the other
day in the city of Melbourne. A poor, nervous
patient, suffering from the follies of early life,
called upon a medical man, who subjected him to
a thorough examination, and then, putting on his
most sympathetic and serious look, exclaimed,
“My poor man, you are in a terrible and danger-
ous condition, your spinal cord is only hanging hj
two threads, and it will cost one hundred and fifty
guineas to cure you.” This announcement of
course frightened the patient out of a year’s life,
and he considered he had better have another
opinion if the case was so serious, and went to
another doctor, who also examined him carefully
and pronounced “ Dr. is a fool, tor
your spinal cord is only hanging by one thread and
it will cost three hundred guineas to cure you.”
And he got it. So much for frightening nervous
subjects, three hundred guineas for a few bottles
of physic not half so good or reliable as Clements
Tonic. This medicine will cure the worst case of
nervous debility when all the quacks have failed
to.“restore manhood;” a course always results
with success, but it must not be expected that one
bottle will give any man a new lease of life. If
your case is worth treating or your life valuable
to you, treat the case thoroughly and faithfully
and you will meet with the same success as others
do. H. H. Bestman, Esq., Klein-street, Too-
woomba, Q. writes : —“ Knowing that you are
always pleased to hear of the cures effected by
your remedy, I am glad to state that I have
derived great results by its use. Some years ago
I caught a severe cold which induced dyspepsia,
severe pains all over my body, the joints being
swollen, my appetite impaired and a great swell-
ing under my right arm, all finally ending in St.
Vitus’ dance, my body being continually ‘ on the
shake.’ I consulted a physician; he prescribed
medicines, hot baths and rubbing the body with
Rhus. Tox, and so on; but his treatment did me
no good. He then had recourse to Clements Tonic
and said I must take a course of that medicine.
I did so and so recovered my appetite, and ray
strength began to improve generally and in eight
months the shakes were entirely stopped, and
now I am entirely cured, solely by the use of
Clements Tonic.” Patients must be on their
guard against imitations ; insist on having what
you call for, “ Clements Tonic.” Refuse all where
the package has been tampered with. E. M.
Clements, Melbourne and Sydney.
Avoid the harsh, irritating, griping compounds
so often sold as purgative medicine, and correct
the irregularities of the bowels by the use of
Ayer’s Cathartic Pills. They are invaluable as a
family medicine.

It is said that the Duke of Clarence madly
loved Princess Helene D’Orleans, daughter of the
Corate do Paris, and that Queen Victoria favoured
the suit, but the Pope would not hear of the lady
becoming a Protestant. It was only after failing
in that quarter that the Duke began to think of
Princess May.
B. R. Wise’s visit to England was osten-
sibly because of certain family obligations, and
common humanity prompted his constituents to
unanimously acquiesce in his proposal. However,
lie didn’t tell the voters that he was going home
in the interests of a capitalistic scheme, or to help
Carington in his electioneering.
Dr. Reunion, Anglican Bishop of Adelaide,
was once curate at Doncaster, and used to be a
great admirer of hoi-se-racing, but didn’t like the
gambling. He once promoted a “ Bishop’s Stake,”
making it a condition that there was no betting
on the race. The enthusiastic parson’s superiors
froze on the idea, and it was abandoned.
A judicial coincidence. Justices Owen
and Manning, first and second Equity Judges of
N.S.W. Supreme Court, live next each other at
Hunter’s Hill. Both are widowers; each has an
only son, and each son is a barrister. The Judges
are steadfast friends, great floriculturists, and
have gardens of almost unequalled beauty.
The house temporarily occupied by the
Marquis of Salisbury, then Lord Robert Cecil, on
bis visit to Bendigo in ’52, is now occupied by
Mr. J. W. Dower, Cambridge Crescent, White
Hills. The Bulletin mentions this for the
benefit of any globe-trotter who denies that Aus-
tralians reverence “historical associations.”
Southward, a life-insurance canvasser,
who disappeared in Maoriland last April, has just
been accounted for. He fell off the summit of
Mount Egmont, and his remains were found the
other day in the snow, with his proposal-form
still grasped in his ice-cold hand and his non-
forfeitable tontine table of rates in his pocket.

In ’5l, W. O. Hodgkinson (now Queens-
land Minister of Mines), then an impecunious
midshipman, bartered the gold lace on his hat for
a few tarts, in Melbourne, and then “ journeyed ”
to Castlemaine. So he says. “Journeyed” is
his own word, and seems somehow too stylish a
means of locomotion to a man who has just eaten
his last tart.
C. J. Dashwood, the new Government
Resident of the Northern Territory, is a bachelor
and 48 years old. He was not a success as a
member of Parliament and would have been
deserted by his constituents in 1893. Dashwood
may make a good Resident, but if he wants to bo
a good Judge (the Resident holds a judgeship) he
will have to brighten up.
Henry Alford, the veteran S. A. policeman,
explorer, and bushman, died last week. He was
one of Tolmer's famous gold escort in 1852- a
body of troopers who brought gold from the Ben-
digo diggers to their families in Adelaide. Tol-
mer’s gold escort saved S.A. from depopulation.
Alford probably caught more criminals and bush-
rangers in his time than any other constable in
Australia.
Warden St. George, now stationed at
Eidsvold, Bananaland, was the officer who offici-
ally opened the Palmer goldfields, 18 years ago,
with about 50 miners, who made their first camp
at Oakey Creek. Three times in the first night
that camp heard the whish of spears in the moon-
light, and three times St. George rounded his men
up, never losing one, to shoot back. The old man
is getting furrowed and bent.

That gkldy shepherd, ex-Pastor Gardiner,
of Sydney, turned up last week in Sydney Insol-
vency Court, where he applied for his certificate.
The Registrar said briefly that the retired
preacher had misappropriated trust-funds and
lost them in wild-cat speculations, that his affi-
davits were unreliable, and that he had exhibited
a shuffling tendency under examination. Conse-
quently expounder G. goes out once more into a
hard world with no certificate.
Alfonso, the dead king of Spain, still lies
on a damp slab in a cave near the old Escurial,
waiting for burial. A thin stream of water
impregnated with lime has run slowly over the
deceased monarch for the last six years, gradually
turning him into a stalactite.; and when he is
properly caked he will be shifted into his regal
tomb, and the slab left vacant for his successor,
and Alfonso will sleep with the long line of pickled
potentates who went before him.
“Mrs. Humphrey Ward, author of
“ Robert Elsmere,” received nearly £20.000 for
her new novel, the “History of David Grieve,”
says the cable-liar. The name of the mad, pub-
lishers? There is no novelist in the world —
unless perhaps Mark Twain, who peddles his own
wares, who would not readily write three novels
to order for £20,000. Mrs. H. W. ought, on the
basis of “Robert Elsmere,” to write ten for the
money. She will be found to be a “one-book”
writer.
The manager of a West Australian bank
has started a Young Men’s Christian Association
at Perth. We have every reason to believe that
the bank is thoroughly sound and the manager
above suspicion, but if we had any money on
deposit we would wake up that bank at midnight
and get it out, or else sit on the doorstep with a
sandwich to keep us up till the office opened in
the morning. The Y.M.C. A. business has proved
fatal to so many banks that we always leave our
money with an infidel now.
Premier Shiels last week received a
deputation in his shirt-sleeves. Minister Bent
once shovelled a load of dirt at Melbourne Harbour
Trust Works whilst going his rounds. John
Thomas Smith, seven times Melbourne’s Mayor,
when Minister of Mines scandalised Turvey-
dropdom by walking down the street shirt-sleeved,
smoking a clay pipe. Another Smith, alias
Carington, once an Australian governor, helped
to publicly shove a railway-truck. These little
performances are supposed to pay dividends.

Bendigo (Vic.) Savings Bank, was the first post-
master on Forest Creek (Castlemaine), and it was
the difficulty of ensuring the regular dispatch of
mails to and their receipt from Bendigo that
induced him to start the first regular mail-coaches
to the latter diggings. Howard was for many
years a representative of Bendigo in Parliament.

There is an Australian in Torquay named
Perkins, whom a good many people in Sydney
will recollect as “Fanny ” Perkins. His mother
kept a bonnet-shop in George-street where Thomp-
son and Giles’ lately stood. His father, he was
heard.to say the other day, bought the land on
which this house stood in 1845 for something like
.£2OOO, and the other day be (Perkins) received
over £lOO,OOO for the same piece of land from the
Government. This person is now swaggering
about spending the income derived from this, and
does not, in all probability, pay a penny to N.S.
Wales in the way of taxation. Worse than this,
though, he does nothing but abuse the country,
which he says is going to Sheol.
A Melbourne journalist tells a pathetic
story of that great man, Melton Priah. He had
some business with Priah rather earlier than
usual one morning, and bursting hurriedly into
his room he found that intellectual giant sitting
on the floor cutting his corns. In order to reach
them more conveniently he had taken off all his
clothes, and was lightly attired in thin fringe of
reddish hair and a pair of spectacles. Anybody
who has ever met Melton Pwiah, or has inhaled
the aroma of his dignity at a short distance, is
invited to contemplate this picture. The general
impression which he always leaves behind him is
that he was born with his bell-topper and his stiff
collar on, and very few could imagine him in the
light of a study in the nude.
This generation of Melbonrnites which
laughs so at Beerthirst Magistrate Panton, doesn't
know that it was largely due to Panton’s tact and
moderation as an assistant to Gold Commissioner
Gilbert that the Bendigo miners did not follow —
or precede—the Ballarat men, and provoke a con-
flict with the authorities. Gilbert and Panton
both discountenanced digger-hunting, and many
a time each of them paid a license-fee out of his
own pocket for some poor devil who would other-
wise have had to go to “ the logs.” Had Bendigo
broken out with the Eureka boys, the authorities
would have gone under. This, Lalor told the
writer, was what Ballarat calculated upon. It
was the knowledge that the whole military force
of the colony could be concentrated on Ballarat
that took the heart out of the “ moral suasionists ”
such as Hurnffray.
The late Robert Macintosh Isaacs, when
N.S.W. Solicitor-General, was once heckling a
witness in a squatting case respecting some mangy
sheep. The witness was very positive and pro-
fessed to be a great authority on scab and mange.
Isaacs, however, posed him by asking if he knew
the difference between poor mangy sheep and sheep
pour manger. Isaacs was (like Salomons, Q.C.,
of to-day) a most tiresome punster. When travel-
ling to Fiji with Sir Hercules Robinson in a man-
o’-war his “humorous” propensities became a
terrible bore, so the ward-room officers conspired
to shut him up. They succeeded. One evening
at dinner Isaacs was in one of his word-twisting
moods, and after a more than usually far-fetched
pun the President called out, “Mr. Vice, a pun,”
and rapped the table three times, saying One,
two, three,” when with one voice the mess roared
out, “Ha! ha! ha!!” The effect was killing.
Isaacs did not make another pun for many a long
day after.
A “great” Australian daily, in its Review
of Literature (written “ at home ”) has the cheek
to say that a certain popular novel now issued in
volume form was first published in its weekly off-
shoot. So it was—and simultaneously in twenty
other syndicate papers.
It was a proud boast of old John Cox, of
Mangoplah, N.S.W., that he had never in his life
knowingly done any man an injury, and those
who knew him could well believe it. Many years
ago, a bank foreclosed a mortgage on the pro-
perty of old Nick B , a well-known settler in
the Yass district. The estate was offered for sale
to Cox, who had always a few thousands to spare.
Upon inspecting the place, it did not take him
long to discover that bad management had been
poor B ’s misfortune. Cox returned to
Wagga, arranged to complete the purchase, and
in due time went to take delivery. He was met
by Mr. B , a fine, picturesque, venerable-
looking old gentleman, who was moved to tears at
having to leave his old home. But old Cox had no
idea of turning him out. He said to B ,
“ Thank God, I never tuk the roof aff av any man’s
house in my life, and I’m not goin’to begin now
I’m an ould man. I’ve bought the place for ye,
and here ye’ll stop. Keep it in the name of God
an’ good look to ye. All the bargain I’ll make is
that I’ll have some hand in the managemint, so ’
that ye’ll not make another mess av it.” He had
been so moved by B ’s tale of woe when he
went to inspect the estate that, without saying a
word to anyone, he made up his mind to buy the
place and allow B to end his days in it. How
many‘people are there who would do the same?
No wonder his friend said over his bones, “ God
bless you John Cox, wherever you are.”
The Cause Revealed.
DISCOVERY OF A SUBTLE AND LIFE
DESTROYING ACID.
Anyone who can go through the trying vicissi-
tudes of this climate without suffering from rheu-
matism or neuralgic pains of some kind is
decidedly fortunate. What is it that causes these
pains has been a greater mystery to physicians and
scientists than almost any other question. Some
have declared that they are the results of a severe
cold, others that imprudent exposure has caused
them, and still others that they arise from blood
poisoning.
In order to obtain, if possible, a correct theory
of rheumatism and all the aches and pains which
accompany it, certain authorities in Washington
have taken steps to obtain the views of the
physicians of America upon this subject. These
views are of a varied nature, but so overwhelming
a proportion hold to one belief to leave but
little doubt that it is the correct one. This belief,
briefly stated, is that uric acid in the blood causes
rheumatism, and that it is only by removing this
poisonous acid that rheumatic or neuralgic
troubles, in all their terrible forms, can be cured.
This being true, the important question arises :
“ How does the uric acid get into the blood, and
how can i t best be removed
Uric Acid finds its way into the blood because
the kidneys are weakened and cannot throw it
from the system. Restore the kidneys, and you
will restore the power that will throw the uric
acid from the system, and thus banish the rheu-
matic agonies which it causes.
This is reason; it is science ; it is true. No one
whose kidneys are in a perfect condition was ever
troubled with rheumatism, and no rheumatic
sufferer, however slight the pain might be, has
perfect kidneys. The conclusion of this truth is
inevitable. Perfect kidneys mean freedom from
rheumatism.
Among the doctors in the various cities of the
United States who have certified to the scientific
statements above mentioned, are a large number
of Boston physicians, among them being :

There never was, and, probably', never will be,
but one remedy that can, in every case, drive uric
acid from the blood. That remedy is Warner’s
Safe Cure. It cures rheumatism by driving the
deadly uric acid from the blood. It banishes pain
by removing the cause. It acts directly upon the
kidneys. Within an hour from the time it is
taken its influence will be felt. It sustains the
organs that sustain life, the forces that make the
blood. For ladies during the severe ordeals through
which they are called to pass it is invaluable. For
the nervous troubles of youth and the debility
that precedes old age it has no rival. There are
thousands of men and women, too, who are in
perfect health to-day wholly by reason of its
great power, and it is the most popular remedy the
world has ever known.

day’s meeting of the Commission the
Committee of Liberal Arts recommended
that plaster busts of the distin-
guished men of New South Wales
should be procured and sent to
Chicago. Seven governors, were sug-
gested, eight premiers and distinguished

A delightful, graphic and biographical sketch of one of
the most remarkable figures of the century.”—Dublin

Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can - be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., >St.
James Chambers, King-street, Sydney.
Society
The Australian Club, Sydney, is just now
(owing to its heavy outlay on its splendidly-
situated new premises) running to the bad finan-
cially at the rate of a couple of thousand a year,
but is, nevertheless, still very chary about whom
it admits to membership. By the way, a little
while ago an eminent Sydney physician of unchal-
lengeable social status put up for membership, but
was blackballed. He had —subsequently explained
his supporters—offended the R.C. Church in days
gone by. A little while afterwards a well-known
Roman Catholic barrister of equally high charac-
ter was nominated —and was enthusiastically
pilled by the doctor’s friends. A truce has since
been declared and both gentlemen have been
elected.
* # *
While Lady Jersey and Lady Galloway
were in Maoriland recently, the train by which
they were travelling from the Lakes to Dunedin
was stuck up by floods at a country place called
Milton. Learning that they would have to pass
a night here, they telegraphed to the local hotel
for rooms. By the same train, though, there
travelled also the members of a second-rate variety
company, including a conjuror, a song-and-danco
artist, and a two-faced, or two-voieed, or two-
something woman, and these also telegraphed for
accommodation to the same hotel as the authoress
of “One People, one Destiny.” The variety
people were the first to arrive at the humble
hostelry, and the unsuspecting proprietor took
them for the Jersey party, and obsequiously
showed them to the best rooms—the only ones he
had left—in his house. Shortly afterwards Lady
Jersey arrived and requested that she should bo
conducted to her rooms. The truth then dawned
on the horror-stricken hotelkeeper and he begged
the variety people to give up their rooms. But
they refused to budge, and their Ladyships of
Jersey and Galloway had to pass the night in the
servants’ bedrooms, the domestics being turned
out to make room for them.
A gilded swell visited Australia in a yacht,
»nd after the manner of his kind, kept himself in
a condition of bright chromo all the time. The
other Sunday morning he took it into his head to
go to church, and, in his mazy mindlessness, put
on his go-to-meeting suit without transferring a
solitary coin to its pockets. During the setvice
he felt fearfully shaky, and, calculating on getting
outside as soon as possible to see a man, made the
awful discovery that he hadn’t a stiver on him.
Just then the collection-plate came round'. He
noticed the lady next to him take out her purse
and extract a shilling therefrom. He had never
seen her before, but be bluntly accosted her with :
—“ Please lend me a shilling to put in the plate?”
What could she do? She gave him the shilling
which he calmly put into his waistcoat-pocket.
And then he went out and got a drink.
* # »
When, just before the final farewell ban-
quet tendered him. Governor Onslow called upon
the Mayor of Dunedin, and incidentally com-
plained of feeling tired, his Worship replied ; “So
am I, your Excellency, awfully tired ; I have been
wa’king about all day, trying to sell tickets for your
banquet .” An ominous silence ensued, during
which you could have heard an elephant fall. At
the banquet, a fussy old potentate asked for and
got three cheers for the Governor. “Now,” says
he, “ three cheers for the Governess !” They were
enthusiastically given.

Lord Kin tore has sent Ids second son to
Eton to be educated. This comes as a surprise.
Taking Adelaide Government House economy as
a “line,” everybody expected that ho would send
the youth to the free State school.
* # #
The standard work on N.S.W. Supreme
Court procedure is “ Pilcher’s Practice.” This
book was really only edited by Pilcher, Q. 0., the
actual compilation was made by Prank .1. Smith
(who last week received seven years’ penal servi-
tude) and another legal gentleman, who not long
since got three years for forgery. From which it
would seem that even lawyers who compile books
for the guidance of others can’t always keep out of
gaol.
# * #
It is said that the reason why the N.S.W.
Minister for Justice has put the ticket-of-leave
system in force is to get some of the lesser
criminals out of the gaols to make room for the
bank and building society brigade. Now that
the Government has so many expert financiers in
its employ, the Treasury books might be handed
over to them to settle the vexed question whether
the Parkes Government left a deficit or a surplus.
# * #
Ex-building-society manager Smairl, of
Sydney, who has just got “ five years hard ” for
embezzlement, played ducks and drakes with
money belonging to near relatives of his, one at
least of whom has been reduced to beggary
through Smairl’s defalcations. The offender
owned a crack yacht and went the pace until he
had reached the end of his tether.
* # *
Dear Bulletin, — John Harris, the de-
ceased millionaire of Shane’s Park, N.S.W., was
a married man after all, and what is more his wife
survives him. They were so long separated and
so far removed from each other that the chances
are the lady doesn’t even know that he is dead —
possibly she never heard of Shane’s Park, and her
husband’s immense pile. What a cold day there
is in store for the other twenty-eight relatives
when she turns up ! —Yours, T.S.

An Ipswich (Q.) man had a case against
the local Small Debts Court bailiff, and obtained
a verdict and costs —in default, levy and distress.
The bailiff made default, and the creditor got an
execution, but the bailiff refused to outer in and
take possession of his own furniture without a
guarantee to cover expenses ! The creditor ap-
pealed to the P.M., who explained that, under
the District Courts Act, the bailiff rendered him-
self liable to be sued for the recovery of a debt
lost by the non execution of an order. The credi-
tor is now in this position—that if the bailiff
declines to seize his own goods in satisfaction of
the verdict, the creditor can sue him again for the
amount lost through his omission and so on ad
infinitum !

Naphthali, a smart Hebrew broker, of
Sydney, owes a local firm —say, Malthazar & Son
—,£so. Repeated applications are made for pay-
ment, but Naphthali heeds them not. Malthazar,
jun., one day, meets Naphthali in the street,
greets him effusively, making no mention of the
unpaid debt, and they take a drink. “Oh,
Naphthali,” suddenly says M., jun., “the old
man wants a diamond ring. That ought to be in
your line. Try and get him a good one, cheap.”
Later, Nap. waits upon the old Malthazar with
“ a beauty. ” The old man, putting the ring on
his little finger : “ How much?” “Oh, .£50.”
“That’s all right.” “Well, then, give us a
cheque?” “ Oh, no,” says old Malthazar. shutting
his fist tightly, “ we don’t do business that way ;
good morning.”

There is an old Sydney deacon as deaf as
an unwilling widow. The other Sunday two
gorgeously-attired swells (male and female) entered
his Ebenezer. Old Auricles, scenting a liberal
“ plate ’’-offering, proceeded to escort them up the
aisle to a front seat. The old man went serenely
onwards, but the visitors, when half-way, quietly
slipped into a vacant pew. Arrived at the
mentally-selected seat, old Auricles turned round,
with a profound bow, and—saw how the congre-
gation did grin !
* # #
The .question now is—What are the tug-
of-war promoters going to do for the man who in
one grim struggle reduced himself by overstraining
to'a complete physical ruin? Also, will they pay
the usual weekly screw to the fifteen or twenty
men who are hors de combat with internal strains
and hemorrhage? The showmen ought to strike
some medals, at least. The rank-and-file who,
seeking to “uphold the honour of their country,”
get shattered and crippled, arc always rewarded
with some form of bauble, ribbon, tin-tag, gim-
crack, whim or jiggumbob.
# * *
Mormon preachers are billed again in
Melbourne. The most curious has been one who
began his career as a walking gentleman at the
Melbourne Queen’s Theatre, acting “Charles, his
friend,” and the like. Then he descended to
become stage-door keeper at another theatre, but
mysteriously disappeared, turning up again, after
a lapse of years, as a Mormon evangelist! But
ho had to go supering again, and appeared as the
good old man at the cathedral door in a Melbourne
Faust ” production.

Boaney’s monogram is ineffaceable on the
iron gates of his Melbourne Cromwell House vet
unlet. “ The great ones of the earth build deso-
late places for themselves.” Redmond Barry user!
to look on the Melbourne Public Library as his
property, and had the impudence to put his coat-
of-arrns, in sculpture, over the door, with the
motto “ Boutez en Avant.” Further, his mono-
gram, in monstrous letters, was let into tl p
pavement of the main hall. For years this was
covered up with a big mat.
At certain seasons of the year there grow
in the narrow flower beds which “lifers” are
allowed to cultivate at Darlinghurst prison a
number of horned snails, which are regarded by
the Jews there as most delectable delicacies.
Issachar and Benoni swallow them as if they were
oysters. Yet Israel never had an oyster in the
saintly days of yore. There is no mention of
molluscs in the Scriptures.

A patent detector money-till has lately
been erected in one of the chief Hobart clubs,
necessity for the same having arisen through
certain “black sheep” having been caught chisel-
ling the billiard-markers out of their shilling
dues. The next step in the right direction will be
keeping a couple of detectives on the premises.
The “suspects,” having been warned off the
grass, have left the club, but the patent detector
till still remains to refresh the memories of the
honourable gentlemen left behind.

Thus a Southern correspondent :
Dear Bulletin, — An artillery-officer was going for a
picnic the other day and told a sergeant that he wanted
four men and a N.C.O. at his quarters at 8.30 a.in. with a
hand-cart to remove some baggage to the wharf. Said
“ hand-cart” weighs a ton and was made to be drawn by
two horses. The baggage consisted of two bundles of
rugs, a tent, two tin-boxes, two fowling-pieces, and a five-
gallon keg of beer. This potentate receives £350 per
year salary and quarters, also an Imperial pension of £3BO,
yet he would not pay a shilling to a carter to remove his
picnic comforts. He would sooner see four poor devils
of men dragging an artillery cart through the streets on
a broiling day.

to travel the Australia.? and drive home to the
hearts of the masses the lesson of the late financial
crashes —that the “most brilliant, financier ” is
the man who can wheedle most of the small
people’s savings into his hands to do as he likes
with, who can scoop the largest profits out of a
“ boom ” founded on falsehood and maintained by
fraud, and who can reveal himself as the meanest,
most contemptible of skunks when the boom col-
lapses, and yet keep out of gaol. “He’s only a
deuced bad financier who gets into gaol ’’—Lord !
what a series of addresses could be delivered from
that text by a strong man ! He’d make another
land boom, with all the concomitants of vicious
luxury, sanctified swindling and gambling, and
robbery of the poor under the guise of “ promoting
national prosperity,” impossible for a full genera-
tion.

Age with thrilling intelligence from London,

an announcement that Blake was alive, in order
to make his death interesting.

THE JERSEY LIST.
Come gather round and shudder now, ye aspirants to
ton,
The Countess, with a darkened brow, has got her eye
upon
The names of some who used to come and taint her marble
hall,
And put away their quarts of Mumm whene’er she gave a
ball.
Her spirit proud abhorred the crowd, there’s not the
slightest doubt,
And so a mighty vow she vowed to weed the duffers out.
List, list, list—she pauses now and then
To put a cross against the names of certain bogey men.
List, list, list, aiid scores of ladies, too,
Will snort and swear and tear their hair—the list she’s
going through.
There’s Dickey Dash, of Manly Beach, with wife and
daughters four,
They used to get a card for each—they’ll never get ’em
in ire;
And Tony Lagge, of Potts’s Point (a “loyal” beast, you
bet!)
His broken nose is out of joint, he’s boosted from “the
set ” ;
And Mrs. Spree, at dear Coogee, will clench her dainty
fist,
For something has been said, you see, and she is off the
list.
List, list, list—a lot of larky dames
Have got the Jersey cross against their fashionable
names.
List, list, list—She’s adding one or two,
But, now and then, she takes her pen and strikes a
dozen through.
AT THE FUNERAL.
To-day we buried 11 Bossy ” Brown,
We gathered round his grave,
And as they let the coffin down
A sort of sigh we gave.
Of all the men we knew in town
He was the biggest knave.
He ne’er performed a kindly deed
As far as any knew,
lie never offered one in need
A solitary sou. ;
But on this sad occasion we’d
A duty “ for to do.”
“ Poor fellow !” said the “ mourning ’’ crowd
As Bossy went to bed ;
Each gazed into the hole and bowed
A cool, uncovered head.
And, all the time, we all allowed
The beast was better dead.
“ Poor fellow !” one must use the word,
’Tis humbug’s solemn whim,
No bosom by his death was stirred,
No eye around was dim—
This secret thought to all occurred,
“ We’ve seen the last of him.”
We bustled off (we’d played our part)
With sober biz’ness air.
Before the train for town would start.
Ten minutes still to spare—
We turned into the Bleeding Hart
And tossed for liquors there.
Melbourne.
E.F

That nice man, the Duke of Marlborough,
writing in the New Review, declares that the
toadies of an English duke are limited to “a few
costermongers or local clergymen.” By coster
mongers he would seem to mean not necessarily
fish hawkers or cats-meat dealers, but low, vulgar
people of the C.M.G. type. Anyway, the exalted
wife-kicker, whose brains are not all in his feet, is
pretty rough on the shepherds.

A painful scene. Melbourne suburban
publican charged with having his bar open on
Sunday. Prosecuting constable (brand-new to
the district) is cross-examined by the presiding
justice (a brother councillor of defendant.) The
Justice (to policeman); “Surely the position of
the door prevented you from having so complete
a view?” Policeman: “ You ought to know how
the bar is situated—you go there often enough
yourself.” Silence in the coort.

just 200. Crime has decreased wonderfully in
S.A. of late years.

Dear Bulletin, —In your “Answers to
Correspondents ” last week you wipe out the
question of feminine equality with one brief and
comprehensive statement: “ A woman is not a
man, nor is a man a woman, and things unlike
cannot be compared. A man and a woman are
no more equivalent quantities than half-a-mile
and 4s. Bd.” All of which is very brilliant no
doubt, and at the same time very irrelevant. “A
man is not a woman nor is a woman a man ”
therefore it is no good talking about the equality
of the sexes ; and, on the other hand, one man is
not another man, neither is another man the first
man, therefore it is no use talking about the
equality of men, and democracy, republicanism,
one-mau-one-vote, and all similar doctrines go to
ruin in one act. The argument you use against
woman is just the argument that the corpulent
Tory of the “ National ” Association uses against
you. Again, “a man and a woman are no more
equivalent quantities than half-a-mile and 4s. Bd.”
Perhaps! Did you ever see half-a-mile eloping
with 4s. Bd., or grovelling before 4s. Bd. and
asking it to marry and be happy for ever after-
wards, or wedding that 4s. Bd. and then loafing on
its money for the balance of its natural life ? Did
you ever see half-a-mile come home drunk and
jump on the miserable 4s. Bd. and stave its ribs in,
or do you know any case when it lived by driving
that exhausted sum of money in the shirt-making
business at 2s. Gd. for sixteen hours’ hard toil? if
man will assume towards woman the same reticent
attitude that the half-mile assumes towards the
fifty-six coppers, and neither associate with it, nor
maltreat it, nor make money out of it, then he has
a right to strike a lofty attitude of superiority,
and then only. Further, you say: “To state
that there are powers and capabilities which men
and women hold in common is to beg the question;
the differences are real and marked and cannot
be bridged over.” You speak here of physical
differences, but are not the mental differences
between (say) the soulless British agricultural
clod and such a writer as Walter Besant equally
marked? Yet you want to have them made equal
by Act of Parliament. This seems rather incon-
gruous.—Yours, Titus Salt,
* # *
Cablegram:—
London, Tuesday Night.—A valuable discovery has
been made at Portaferry, a seaport and market town in
County Down, Ireland. While a grave was being dug a
sum of £20,000 in gold was unearthed.
This kind of thing—“A valuable discovery has
been made,” &c. —is an insult to human intelli-
gence. Ten thousand in gold had been found in a
grave, and to solemnly set forth in long primer
that it was “a valuable discovery” is to assert
that this avaricious and grasping nation hasn’t
enough sense to come in out of the wet. Boil it
down, boil it down !

giving him a chance to run for it. It is just the
same as howling at a dog that is fastened with a
short chain —the lunatic has no show. In one of
Mrs. Beecher-Stowe’s novels there is a story of a
well-meaning but horribly dreary man who talked
for two hours and a half to a paralysed woman on
the necessity for calmness and resignation. The
unfortunate audience had only sufficient power to
move her eye-lids and her jaws; otherwise she
was helpless as well as speechless. At the end of
the two and a half hours “she sorter beckoned
with her eye ” to the well-meaning man, and when
he came close up she bit a good-sized piece out of
him. We commend this item to the consideration
of asylum chaplains—and of asylum lunatics.
* * *
Somebody owns a dove-cote in the imme-
diate vicinity of the Sydney divorce-court, and a
pleasant liquid sound of “ Coo-00-oo ” comes in at
the window, and mingles with the story of how
the respondent kicked the petitioner, or how the
witness saw the co-respondent getting out through
the scullery-door at midnight. It is a pleasant
sound, and very suggestive of the way the peti-
tioner and the respondent carried on in the days
when they were young and innocent, and before
either of them got acquainted with the co-respon-
dent, or began to sue each other for judicial
separation and maintenance.

When the mail left there were 300,000
people ill in London, most of them from influenza
alone. No less than 159 deaths were announced
in the Times of Jan. 15. It is only by substi-
tuting the name of “influenza” for “plague
that a panic has been avoided.

# * *
People have been asking why Mr. E. W.
Hawker, himself wealthy and the son of an
immensely wealthy man (the Hon. G. C. Hawker),
should run round the globe picking up scraps or
metallurgical knowledge. The explanation is said
to be the existence of promising silver-lodes on
their great freehold estates at Bungaree, n. A. It
so, how passing strange is the unceasing search of
the rich man for “more greed !”
* * *
It is the correct thing now in high-toned
American society to rehearse a wedding over and
over again before the actual ceremony comes on,
so that all parties concerned may be perfect in
their parts. The bride and bridegroom practise
kneeling down at the altar, getting up again,
fixing on the ring, and all other details. They go
over the entrance and the exit from the church,
and walk up the aisle at different rates of speed
till they strike the one that seems most dignified.
The bride gets up assorted expressions of counten-
ance, and her friends and relatives decide whether
she should come in looking pale or wearing a
blush, whether she should look nervous and timid,
or calm and self-possessed, or whether she should
wear a smile. They rehearse positions at the
altar —leaning forward, standing erect, Grecian
bends, etc., and make the responses in various
tones till they hit the right one. Then by the
time the real ceremony comes on all the details
are correct, and all the romance has been trodden
out of the thing, and everything has become as
commonplace and prosaic as a mud-bank on a wet
day, and it is hardly worth while getting married
at all.

Lord Sheffield has returned to England.
Explanation—the Australian summer. He won a
good name in his travels, and many cricketers
affirm that had he been present at the last N.S.W.
match, W.G. would not have violated the rules of
cricket and decent behaviour by directly insulting
Umpire Briscoe during the game.
# * *
If there is a good-natured, accommodating,
kindly, and absolutely unselfish ass on earth,
it is surely the man who will write to a paper
stating that ho is in the habit of associating on
familiar terms with the gaol-flogger, and that he
likes the flogger, in a social sort of way, as a com-
panion, and feels better for the flogger’s society.
An alderman of Woollahra (Sydney) is just this
sort of ass, as witness the following letter, pur-
porting to come from him, in Sydney Tboih :
In justice to a man I consider being persecuted, I would
like to say a few words. I hare known Ellory personally
for some years, have been in bis company frequently, and
had numerous business transactions with him. lamin a
position to prove that lie numbers amongst those who
respect him—as I do—gentlemen of position and integrity
in this city', and I am certain they will bear me out that
he bears a character for respectability, honesty, sobriety,
and principle that not many warders or officers need he
ashamed of. It is because 1 know him as such that I take
this opportunity of defending a man I deem is being per-
secuted.
In other words, this alderman is prepared to vouch
that Mr. Ellory is a kind-hearted flogger, a
thoroughly honourable and gentlemanly scarifier,
and is much respected by all who know him—but
who haven’t been flogged by him—as a high-
principled remover-of-skin and flesh-agitator. And
now The Bulletin would like to know, merely as
a matter of curiosity, whether the alderman ever
asks the hangman to drop in to tea with his noose
sticking out of his pocket, and, if so, does lie
invite the hangman and the flogger on the same
evening, and in that case what do they converse
about ? Also, how does the hangman figure in
the giddy valse at the alderman's suburban man-
sion, and how is the flogger on the light fantastic
toe?
A Funny Game.
A few friends met the other night in a Newtown
store to spend a jovial evening, but as they were
all teetotallers and none of them drank anything
stronger than Clements Tonic, drunkenness was
out of the question. Mac. proposed a game
at euchre, and Jones said they had better play for
something just to give the game an interest and
make it lively, but times were hard, so they did
not care to play for money. Smith, however,
spied a box of Fletcher's bilious and liver pills,
and proposed that each time a man was beaten he
should take a pill, and this made the game lively
and the fun was fast and furious. Simpson
swallowed five, Jones got away with four and
Smith and Hopkins two each and Mac. got off
scot free. The game terminated by a general
exodus at 3 a.m., for Dr. Fletcher’s pills never
labour in vain. They are the best and mildest
pills made and are sold by all chemists and
druggists everywhere; being a positive, safe,
and reliable cure for constipation, liver complaint,
headache, &c. They always give satisfaction.
The proprietors give away £5O (in seven prizes) at
Xmas, for the seven first nearest guesses sent in,
as to how many of these pills a large Clements
Tonic bottle (now full) holds. A guess form is
around every box, and all such forms are eligible
for this competition that come from around a box.
One box of Fletcher’s Pills proves their virtues
and they can be obtained everywhere at Is., or
post free 13 penny stamps, from F. M. Clements,
Newtown, N.S.W.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Providence, which always seems to smile upon
the V.R.C., sent glorious weather for the Newmarket
handicap day at Flemington, and the general attendance
was about large enough to meet the Club’s heavy outlay
in stakes and hairpins. The Governor managed to get
there, in spite of the new baby, and most of the represen-
tative persons who haven’t called a meeting of creditors
were there likewise, without their wives. It looked, in
fact, as though many of the best ladies of quality had
stopped at home and gone to bed out of sympathy. Very
likely they had. When the famous Duke of Burgundy
became as bald as an egg his courtiers shaved their heads,
and Lady Hopetoun’s predicament is much more interest-
ing and worthy of imitation than the Duke’s. Which is a
delicate reminder that sport opened with the Hopeful
Stakes, a “dead-bird” for Long’s stylish filly, Lady Ed da.
The admirers of Strathmore were not enraptured with the
Derby winner’s appearance as he stood stripped in the pad-
dock. The coat that had bloomed in the Spring was not so
bright-looking on Saturday, and the great horse seemed
in danger of being taken dow n for the Leger by Oxide,
Until one took observation of the latter. Oxide, appar-
ently, was also a trifle off-colour, and so the two horses
ran relatively in their old form, La Tosca being an easy
third, and the long • legged impostor, Swordbearer,
sprawling in the rear. Thirty-three horses—a monotonous
mob of well-trained cattle for the poor punter to select
from—came out for the Newmarket. Half of them were
certain to win and the rest couldn’t lose. On paper
Bungehah should have done it, inasmuch as he won the
same race by twenty yards last year, and the extra
weight he now- put up could make little difference to
the biggest horse in the paddock. Moreover, Bungy
looked so muscular and chirpy that people pushed for
the privilege of backing him for a place. The difference
between one Newmarket and another obtrudes itself right
here. The speed that seemed so marvellous last year
was, on this occasion, barely sufficient to keep Bungy in
the front rank, wherefore the veteran lost heart and
finished seventh. Wild Rose, who won a good stake for
the G’Naroo stable, has shown some wonderful times
before Saturday, but she doesn’t quite seem to fill the
stage as a Newmarket star. Fortunatus, the runner-up,
is only a pony, and Harbour Light, who finished within
two lengths of the winner, has suffered about as
many defeats, in moderate company-, as there
are hairs in his tail. It seems odd that the
Wilson party, with such splendid. appliances at
hand for testing Pygmalion’s character, could back the
horse for a heap of money without ever getting a ghost of
a show of winning it, but they did. Pyg. may have a day
out during the meeting, but he is an expensive beast to
follow, anyway. Zalinski, on the other hand, will always
be w orth ba -king whilst he has four legs to travel on.
The Trenton blood is in him, and he is never beaten until
the post is passed. On Saturday it was plain that Zalin-
ski wasn’t quite “fit ” in the sense of being “thoroughly
wound up,” but he was fit enough to beat Highborn,
Megaphone, Stromboli and Tirailleur at weight for age,
and will improve on that gallop as the week goes on.

Ned Lord and, young Landale, both of
Australia, are rowing in the Cambridge boat
on trial. This is not the Landale who rowed
last year, but another brother, making the third of
that stalwart family who have donned the light blue.
Ned Lord will probably row at Putney-, but the other is
not certain. It is possible that J. C. Garrick may be
tried. Experts predict already- a win for Oxford, who
have so many old hands, while the Cantabs will have at
most three old blues. But you never can tell.

The V.R.C. has some notion of reviving an
agitation to get the privilege of charging for every- visitor
to Flemington course. Act of Parliament now stops it,
and a few- years ago the club committee vainly attempted
to persuade Government to bring in a Bill handing them
entire control, with a view to charging. On a big Cup
day this would make a difference of £2OOO, at a shilling a
head, but the Amateur Turf Club charges 2s. at Caulfield.
The case is the same as with the M.C.C., which had to
exercise years of intrigue ere it could secure the right of
excluding the people from their own park.

* » •
The secretary of a Victorian cricket club tells
this painful story-: He called on a generous M.L.C. to
request a subscription for the club, and the M.L.O.
promptly produced a sovereign. The secretary didn’t
like to rush on the coin like a famished beast of prey, so
he sat and chatted for a few minutes with the money
still on the table. Presently there was a rustling of skirts
outside the door, and the M.L.O. sat up in alarm, and
gesticulated eagerly to intimate that he should seize the
coin. At the same moment a lady entered—a dried-up
sort of lady with yellow features and an old and haggard
eye. The sovereign was the first thing that drew her
attention. “What’s that?” she demanded promptly-.
“It’s only a small donation,”said the husband, uneasily ;
“The local cricket club, my dear.” “Oh!” said the
lady, “ Half-a-crown’s enough.” And then, before
the eyes of the astonished secretary, she peacefully
annexed the sovereign, fished out half-a-crown from the
recesses of her clothes instead, and went away. The
secretary takes no more chances now. If he sees even a
sixpence he flies at it like a vulture rushing on a dead
body, lest some other woman should walk in and say that
twopence is sufficient.

Dear Bulletin,—l’m astonished to see a
democratic paper countenancing two classes of amateurs
—bonafide and manual labour. The distinction is un-
known outside Australia. What is wanted is the enforce-
ment of the true amateur principle, which would exclude
the “ amateur” who follows the sport for gain. To say
that manual labourers are physically at an advantage is
absurd. At pedestrianism the well-nourished college
novice with elastic joints is superior to the youth whose
limbs are stiffened by hard fare and harder toil. Only by
continuous practice does the pro. overtake the amateur
whose training is by starts and bursts. This question
reminds me of the old Henley definition and London,
A. wrangle when the (wholesale) “gentlemen
engaged in commerce” would not meet the (re-
tail) “fellows employed in trade.” Plow poor
Pendragon used to sneer 1 To their credit be it said, the
young swells of Oxford and Cambridge ended the despic-
able quarrel, by volunteering to meet the humblest
athlete, provided he followed the sport for sport's sake
only. The new movement was inaugurated in 1831, when
Raby, a farm labourer, won the walk, and three ’V arsity
men had the hurdles to themselves, our friend Tom
Malone, then a bhoy from Clare, was a good second to
Davin iu the long jump, and B. R. Wise won the mile.
B. worked hard to abolish all social distinctions
between true amateurs. No wonder that he, in disgust,
a few years ago, refused the presidency of a Sydney club
which raised the manual-labour barrier. —Yours, V.

The largest fees ever received by jurymen
in Australia were paid to those who sat on the
great railway case, Cornish and Bruce v. The
Queen, trial at Bar, Melbourne, in the sixties.
Each man received six guineas per day for 42
days’ sittings. George Higinbotham appeared
for the Queen, with R. A. Billing. The other
side secured Michie, Ireland, Fellows, and all the
great lights of the then magnificent Victorian
Bar. The verdict was for the plaintiffs, nominally
for 405., really for over half a million sterling.
Dear Bulletin, — A “toff” shearer, popu-
larly credited with being nephew to a bishop and
a bank-manager and the son of no end of a swell,
shore (scab, of course) at a station in the Namoi
country, but was “roomed” and “fed apart”
from the common ruck. Some money, a bank-
draft and an order, &c., amounting in all to
over £3O, belonging to a unionist shearer, were
stolen from the office cashbox where they had
been deposited for safety, and the amount had to
be made up by the two gentlemen who had it in
charge. Several honest shearers were suspected
of the mean theft, and much' enmity and uneasiness
were caused among hitherto mates. At length,
suspicion centred on the “toff,” who was plainly
informed of the fact, and given every chance of
restitution, but his aristocratic soul preferred that
others should remain under a cloud as well as him-
self. However, a bobby bailed him up while
aboard the outward-bound coach, and the loot
was found on him—notes endorsed by the loser-
concealed in his boot. The “toff” was presented
at the nearest police-court. Now, a near
relative of the prisoner drove the emergency
magistrate over to the court, and doubtless
the two fed together on the road. The magis-
trate let the aristocratic prisoner off with
a fine of .£2O, and delivered him over
to his friends in good condition. At
the end of the case, prosecutor audibly expressed
his opinion that the “toff” had been unduly
favoured, and that, had the thief been a common
worker, without influential friends, he would
have seen what gaol life was like. The magis
trate at once suggested “contempt of court,” and,
had not an unusually intelligent constable in-
formed his worship that “contempt” couldn’t
“lie” after the case had been disposed of —
though, whether that is law or not, seems
open to question—the prosecutor would probably
have had to study cell walls for seven
days, for expressing his opinion honestly. Will
Tub Bulletin try and procure a definite and
authoritative decision as to whether the posses-
sion of influential, rich, and holy relatives carries
with it the further privilege of exemption from
the punishments that are in all cases heaped upon
the heads of the erring who have neither bishops,
nor bankers, nor influence to back them up?—
Yours, &c., Shearers’ Probity.
Over 1500 foxes have been destroyed in
Victoria since 1883. They are nevertheless so on
the increase that the associated farmers have
agreed to offer ss. a skin reward.
An English newspaper interview on the
toe-nail question ;
The toe-nails can be as much improved as the nails of
the fingers. It’s a pity girls don’t take a little more care
before they get engaged. It takes a good three months
to get the average toe-nails into presentable shape. We
always find our customers impatient to be guaranteed
“all right” in such and such a time. They are, of
course, being pressed to name the day, and they cannot
do it, as, of course, you can understand, till their toes are
as nearly faultless as possible —they are far from it, as a
rule. Dark stockings have been worn so long that ladies
have come to understand that “ stain ” is not necessarily
soil. It is just as unsightly, though. All this must be
got away, the toe-nails bleached and then tinted, and as
far as possible some beauty of shaping given. Modern
custom requires the wearing of shoes too small.
Of course, the toes must suffer, in beauty as
well as iu other ways. The nails get cramped,
distorted, discoloured and very ugly. What do
you do ? It takes an hour or so a day. The foot is
kneaded and rubbed, the nails carefully cut into shape,
the “ moons ” coaxed to appear—just as on the fingers.
Then home treatment is dictated. Girls sleep with their
feet bandaged, with bleaching and softening preparations,
with their toes bound upon splints, and their ankles
shaped by binding. We, of course, can only accomplish
w'hat the foot permits. We can make the skin soft,
smooth and white ; give, perhaps, some plumpness where
it is needed, and make the soles soft and pink, give tint
to the tiplqpf the toes and to the nails, make the heels
rosy, and bring perhaps all the moons in sight if we have
time. A cared-for foot is, I assure you, a thing of beauty.
It’s one of the first things a girl looks after when she gets
engaged.
THIS WILL SETTLE THE QUESTION.
“ I don't believe there is a word of truth in it.”
That’s the way certain foolish people talk when
they hear of anything unusual, or outside the
limits of their own observation or experience.
They are of the sort who laughed at Stephenson
when he introduced the railway, and at Morse
when he said that we could communicate by means
of electricity. Yet they don’t laugh at those
things now. They make use of them daily.
Some months ago the case of Mrs. Mary Cuddy
was first published. The great public accepted
the facts, as they had every reason to do. Others,
a very few (professional men perhaps) pretended
to doubt, and sent emissaries to inquire of the
lady herself whether her allegations had not been
added to, or altered, for the sake of popular effect.
Here is her answer! She repeats what she at
first said, and puts a quietus on all who called
her words in question. It will be observed that
her statement is as plain and solemn as language
can make it.
[copy.]
I, Mary Cuddy, of 28, Catherine Street, Rich-
mond Road, Leeds, do solemnly and sincerely
declare as follows :
Ever since I was a girl I have suffered from ill-
ness. I always had a pain both before and after
eating, and never seemed able to gain and keep
my strength, and felt that something was pulling
me down. I had a nasty queer feeling in my
stomach. Sometimes food seemed to ease it, and
at other times it made me feel worse, and often I
went without food, for I was afraid to eat. Com-
monly when food was placed before me I could not
touch it, and I often fainted at the very sight of it.
After a while I became so weak I could scarcely
stand or walk. I thought it was consumption
coming on by degrees and I took all sorts of
medicines to try and get relief, but it was of no use
and I got tired of taking physic, for I had lost all
faith in it. My business was so urgent that I was
compelled to be at work, otherwise-I would have
laid in bed, so weak had I become. With the
weakness and loss of appetite there were other
feelings and signs that were bad and alarmed me
greatly. Among them were these : —A yellowish
colour of the skin and eyes, sometimes a cold,
clammy perspiration, pains and aches in the sides,
the chest and back, headache, a kind of wind or gas
coming up into my throat and mouth that was so
sour and sickening I could scarcely bear it. Once in
a while I would have a strange fluttering and pal-
pitation that made me think my heart must be
affected. My heart would thump so that I feared
it would jump out of its place, and I have had to
walk about the room for two or three hours at a
time, for I could not sit or lie. The pain was so
severe that I have asked my husband if he could
not hear my heart thumping as I walked about.
I always slept badly at night, and frequently had
horrible dreams, and was so melancholy and
depressed in spirits that I would sit down and
cry, for I got no pleasure as time dragged wearily
by. I had so little energy or strength that it was
all I could do to summon courage for the labour
upon which the family (at least in part) depended
for support. I am a dressmaker, and it will be
easily understood how hard my life was, for I
didn’t think it would last much longer. Not long
ago (May, 1887) I made up my mind to try a
medicine that is advertised and known all oyer
the country. I mean Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup. I had no faith in it at first, for how can
one believe in what one knows nothing about? I
bought and tried Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup
only because of its reputation. How could so
many people, I asked myself, praise a medicine so
much if it had no virtue ? I can only say that I
found what they said to be true. After beginning
with the Syrup, relief soon followed. My food
digested better and gave me strength, and by
persevering with it all my pains disappeared. I
could eat my food with a relish, and everything
agreed with me. Now and again when, through
confinement and hard work, I feel a touch of my
old complaint I take a dose or two of Seigel’s
Syrup and the trouble goes no further. Since the
publication of my testimonial many persons have
called at my house anil asked me if all that is pub-
lished about my case is true, and if the Proprietors
of Seiycl’s Syrup had made, additions to my state-
ment.
I told them all that every toord teas true and
nothing had been added by the Proprietors of the
medicine, but I could add a good deal more, for no
words can describe what my sufferings were
during all those long years. I never expected
being well again in this world. Seigel’s Syrup
saved my life and I desire other sufferers to know
of what did so much for me. I will gladly answer
inquiries.
And I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, by
virtue of the provisions of the Statutory Declara-
tion Act (Will. IV., c. 62.)
(Signed) Mary Cuddy.
Declared before me at Leeds, in the
County of York, by the said Mary
Cuddy, on Monday, the 10th day of I
August, 1891. (
(Signed) Alf, Cooke,
Mayor of Leeds. J
Not a syllable further is needed except to say
that her ailment, indigestion and dyspepsia,
burdens and saddens the lives of many other
women (and men also), who will read with new
hope the outcome of Mrs. Cuddy’s case, and place
a confidence which no cavilling can shake, in the
remedy which restored to her the health and
happiness that Providence designs for us all.
Vincent Pyke, one of The

torian Parliament the Bill for establishing a
