.£3 17s. per oz,
A certain Sydney “banker,” who has just
gone bung, started business with .£15,000 lent him
by a retired tailor, the bargain between them
being that he was to pay the tailor 15 per cent,
and deposit with him the securities on which he
lent the money ; anything he could make over
15 per cent, was to be his own. He did so well
that he was soon able to dispense with the tailor’s
patronage, but presently he started out, like all
the rest of them, to own the entire earth, of which
he acquired, to speak allegorically, all but about
320 acres. In endeavouring to get this last half-
section he broke his financial back.
When the newspaper man retires from
business a certain halo still hangs around him, and
if he was a well-known individual casual drinks
come in his way for five years afterwards on the
strength of his old position. The other day a
Sydney man, who used to be among the best-
known journalists in the city, came across the
owner of one of the many forlorn, dead-looking
“big buildings” that have sprung up of late
years, and began talking. “That place of yours
must be an awful white elephant,” he remarked.
“ White elephant! Bless you, no—have a drink !
—grand place ! Just let me show you through
it.” They had a small bottle of champagne
each (at the landlord’s expense—of course), and
then they went through it. “ This,” said the
genial owner waving his hands in the air in a
general sort of way, “is probably the finest site
in the city—have another drink.” They had
another small bottle of champagne each, and then
they continued to go over the property. They
shinned up to the roof, and scrambled down to the
basement, and dodged through all sorts of places.
“ Grand central situation, as you may observe,”
said the proprietor cheerfully, “ commanding
position—have a cigar.” They had a shilling
cigar each, and then they started afresh. “ The
capabilities of a property like this are absolutely
boundless—let’s have another big bottle —it only
requires a little'assistance from men like you to
make it known.” “ From men like me! ” said the
ex-journalist, lifting his open countenance out of
his third Dint of fizz; “How can I make it
known, pray?” “Why, of course, you know,
men occupying an influential position on the
press, you know ■” “I’m not on the press,”
said the retired writer, promptly. “ Not on the
press !” said the owner, in a wild, give-me-back-
my-expensive-drinks, where-is-my-vanished-cigar
sort of tone: “Why, aren’t you on the ?’
“Left the press five years ago,” interposed the
recipient of champagne. “But, anyhow, you
have influence in newspaper circles, through your
long connection with them, and a word from you
” “Haven’t any influence,” said the ex-
newspaper man curtly. “But still,” said the
other, in a despairing lone, as he thought of the
vanished drinks, “You meet lots of press-
men ” “ Never meet one of them ; haven't
met a pressman since 1888.” “But you must
know them.” “No, I don’t know any of them.”
“Oh, well, good-day,” said the fat landlord, in
sad, stomach-pump tones.

beer case, before the Melbourne Courts the other
day, was an advertising dodge? Even a sort of

“ divorce salary,”

Swedish sailor, who, it is alleged, was deliberately
murdered by larrikins in George-street, Sydney,
lately, did not meet with his death through fall-
ing from the steps of the street “ offices ” outside
of which he was found.

Police became aware that a brace of detectives,
prosecuting in a criminal case, raised £25 by
getting their own joint personal bill, without
other security than their names, discounted by
the lawyer defending in the same criminal case—
what would he do in the matter ?

manager of the New York Life Insurance Co.,
Mr. Josiah Howell, is informed that the total
assets of the Company at Ist January, 1892, are
■£25,915,080, a gain during the year of over
£2,000,000, with a clear surplus over liabilities of
£3, 105,432. The total income for the year
(>£0,554,300) and the new business written
amounted to .£31,000,000, while the expenses have
decreased by over £lOO,OOO.
Mr. Josiah Howell, general manager for
Australasia of the New York Life Insurance
Company, is officially informed by cable that the
position of president of that company, rendered
vacant through the resignation of Mr. Wm. H.
Beers, has been filled by the unanimous election
of Hon. John A. M‘Call. The appointment
of Mr. M'Call, formerly Insurance Commissioner
of the State of New York, gives great satisfaction,
as his past record gives guarantee that he will
prove a worthy successor to the retiring officer in
safe-guarding the interests of the policyholders in
the great company over whose destiny he has been
chosen to preside.

OUR DARLING BABY
WHAT IT IS CAPABLE OF
Has it ever struck you how many things a baby
can do and feel none the worse for it? It can turn
a once peaceful and happy home into a howling
wilderness, and then smile in the face of its suffer-
ing father just when he is in the act of pulling up
his great coat collar under his ear preparatory to
going out in the cold night to keep an imaginary
appointment in the city. It can occupy both
sides of the largest-sized bed simultaneously with-
out the slightest discomfort to itself. It can
make itself look like a fiend just at the moment
when mamma wants to show “ what a pretty baby
she has.” It can look its father innocently in the
face, and five seconds later spoil the only good
coat that he has got in the world. It can make
an old bachelor in the house adjoining use
language that if uttered on the street would land
him in gaol for a month. It can go to sleep like a
little angel, and just as papa and mumma are
starting for the theatre it can wake up and stay
awake until the beginning of the last act. But
that is only one side of the question. A baby can
make the commonest home the brightest spot on
earth. It can lighten the burdens of a loving
mother’s life by adding to them. It can flatten
its dirty little face against the window pane in
such a way that the tired father can see it as a
picture before he rounds the corner. Another
source of gladness to every well-ordered household
is a bottle of the world-famed St. Jacobs Oil, a
family medicine that has done more to relieve
human suffering than all the other patent nostrums
ever placed in the market. From amongst
numberless testimonials recently received attest-
ing the efficacy of St. Jacobs Oil in the relief and
cure of such diseases as rheumatism, backache,
headache, neuralgia, toothache, sprains, bruises,
cuts, &c., the following by Mr. A. T. Burton, of
Concord Park, near Sydney, is worth perusal.
He says;—“l consider St. Jacobs Oil a most
valuable remedy, and always keep a supply of it
in the house. In cases of rheumatism, neuralgia,
and sprains, and aches of all kinds, it relieves and
soothes the pain. About 18 months ago I sprained
my arm, when I used the oil to good effect. It
relieved the pain at once, and after a few applica-
tions effected a complete cure.”

can ever become a permanent success unless it
possesses sterling merit. That the famed Water-
bury watches possess merit of the highest degree
is borne out by the enormously increasing demand
for them, and the spontaneous, genuine testi-
monials which pour in from wearers of them daily.
Mr. J. R. Filewood, of Fernbank, Shoalhaven
district, writes : “ I have worn one of your short-
wind watches (series “J”) for over 18 months,
and I would not exchange it for any watch I ever
saw I have worn it out camping
in the wet and working on the goldfields ; and it

series, “ K ” for gentlemen, and “N ” for ladies,

A meeting of fishermen and fish-dealers
was held in Melbourne lately with the view of
establishing a co-operative society to break down
the present fish monopoly. One of the speakers
suggested that when formed the society should
“keep clear of members of Parliament ”
The poor Victorian 51. L. A.
Gets snubs on all sides, of extreme variety ;
Just fancy being, as they say,
Too fishy for a Fisherman’s Society !
Judge Sir W. C. Windeyer’s father is
buried in the Elphin Rood cemetery, Launceston,
Tas. Stand at the foot of his grave, and you can
throw biscuits on to the respective tombs of the
man whose coin helped to found Victoria ; of a
man who was a member of the South Australian
Company which established South Australia ; of
a man who suggested the formation and was one
of the first directors of the Union Bank of Aus-
tralia ; of a man who landed in New South Wales
at so early an age that his nurse was a “First
Fleet ” transport; of a man who was in Maoriland
in 1829 ; and of a man who narrowly escaped
hanging, in ’9B, alongside of the Sheares.

H. GRAYSON CROSSLY .... General Manager.
Keaa-‘A Golden Shanty.’
John Wesley the Snag.
(For THE BULLETIN.)
The quaint name which heads this sketch
belonged to a picturesque-looking old chief of the
Ngatimaniapoto tribe, Maorihmd. In its native
form it was Hone Wetere Te Rerenga, of which
John Wesley the Snag is a literal translation. Hone
had been “caught young” by the missionaries,
and, in addition to his tribal name, had received
by baptism the name of the founder of Methodism.
But the nomenclature carried with it no saving
grace, as Hone was just about as “bad” a Maori
as you could have found if you iiad raked all Maori-
hand from Capo Maria van Dieman to Foveaux
Straits. His chief Biblical hero was Joshua, but
he had his doubts as to whether the sun really
stood still. He scouted the portions of holy writ
relating to Jonah and the whale, and the Creation
and the Nativity as unworthy of credence. Ho
was a kai tanyatci (man-eater or cannibal), an eye-
swallower, a devotee of the Pai-manri supersti-
tion, a rebel against Kuinc Wikitoria, had mur-
dered Missionary Whiteley (grandfather of
Whiteley King, of the N.S.W. Pastoralists’
Union, by the way) and drunk his blood —and was,
in fact, everything that was undesirable in the
eyes of the orthodox pakeha.
* # #
I first met Hone in 1884 in a wild, out-of-
the-way part of his ancestral demesne. He had
then been under the ban of outlawry for 20 years
and there had been a standing reward of,a couple
of hundred pounds on his tattooed head all that
time, but as his retreat was almost inaccessible
and his henchmen numerous, well-armed and
valorous, few and half-hearted had been the
attempts made to earn the blood-money. As he
was an uncatehable outlaw a vast tract of country
was tabooed to the settler through his outlawry,
and at last Native Minister Bryce decided to
grant an amnesty to Hone and all other Maori
offenders who would promise to obey the law in
future. Hone jumped at the chance of pardon
with its concomitant advantages of unrestricted
visits to the pubs, of frontier villages, &c.. and
displayed as great a desire to become a “good”
Maori as he had previously exhibited to bo a bad
one; the Government, to encourage him in well-
doing, actually gave him a position in the Civil
Service—as pilot at the Mokau River. It wasn’t
a very responsible billet, and the emolument
attaching to it was only some .£4O a year and a
brass-bound c?ip, but I verily believe that Hone
thought more of bis new title of Captain than
he did of his tribal mana and his un-
challengeable ancestry of thirty generations.
# # #
The occasion of my foregathering with Wetere
was a visit paid by several members of the
Government to his territory—a journey made with
the object of showing that the authorities
regarded his submission to the pakeha as sincere.
The chief wore his staring new naval cap, an old
serge coat, and a blanket around his loins, dis-
playing his brawny, copper-coloured legs, from
knee down. He took a violent fancy to me right
off, squatted on his hams in front of me, and com-
menced the process of chumming up, “ Kapai
God !” he ejaculated. I smiled faintly and bowed
affirmatively. “ Kapai Jesy Keriste.” I remarked
that I thought so too. Then he dug down into
his Scriptural recollections and “ Kapai’d ” Adam
and Noah, and then Solomon, and Sir George
Grey, and Bishop Hadfield and Queen Victoria,
and all the persons alive and dead that
he thought my pakeha susceptibilities would
cause me to admire. Then he sidled up close
to me and whispered, “But no good,
Mita Miterahena (Mr. Mitchelson, the Minister
for Works). He wantee mo join the - prn
ribbon army.” Wetere might have continued his
confidences further, had not one of his followers
informed him that I was not the Governor, but
merely a tan rika rika of a reporter. His interest
in me suddenly ceased when he found that I was
not a ranyatirn, and a few seconds later he was
“ kapai ”-ing the God head and the Guelph family
to the Minister for Public Works.
G.J.S.

A JUST REWARD.
Herb Rassmussen, of whose Famous Herbal Remedies everybody
has beard, is yet a young man, and has held the position of Lecturer
to several botanic Societies in Australia , be soon became known by
his wonderful cures of which he has numerous tokens of grateful-
ness, including a beautiful Gold Medal, brought him into the front
rank of Botanic Scientists ; now he is the Proprietor and Discoverer
of the famous Denmark Herbal Remedies, all of which have
secured the honest support of the people, not only of Australia,
but Africa, the United States, the British Kingdom and Italy.
It is not for us to enumerate the various remedies prepared by this
really learned man, but having seen and examined letters from all
parts of the world, we are assured that he has saved a world of
suffering and cured people of many complaints, after they had
given up all hope.”— The Australian Workman.— Sydney,
January 9th, 1892.

Statistician Hayter : There are ten per-
sons in Victoria whose ages range from 100 to 109
years.
England imports annually some £3,400,000
worth of margarine. The bulk of this bogus
butter comes from France.
During ’9l the paper values— market
quotations—of shares in Australian Joint Stock
Companies declined to the enormous total of 25
millions sterling.
Ambrose Bierce says civilisation consists
in having one wife and several mistresses, and in
holding up one’s hands in horror at the wretch
who commits bigamy.
The fascinatingly blue lakes at Mount
Gambier (S.A.) are just now as rapidly and
steadily falling as a short time ago they rose.
Eighteen indies in two or three weeks has been
recorded, and three inches in one instance in two
days. Natural evaporation can account for
nothing like this in such large volumes of
“ plumbless waters.”
The case of Gustav Adolf Streseman, who
turned up at Vanrook-station, Gulf country, after
being bushed 13 days without provisions, and who
was naked and blistered and so ravenous that he
fell tooth and nail upon a batch of uncooked
dough, is another illustration of the fact that it is
quite a usual symptom of famine-madness for the
Victim to tear off his clothing as if it oppress d
him beyond endurance.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
Ah Sin was up in Queensland recently for
stealing two water-fowl from a stupid Quaker.
“When did you miss the two ducks?” asked
Police Sub-inspector O’Driscoll, pointing to the
fowl in Court. Quaker: “I missed only one
duck, your Worship.” O’D. : “ You cannot
understand the question. You have identified
these two ducks as your property.” Quaker
(seriously): “ One of them is a drake.” Even the
Chow grinned.

QUEENSLAND.
NOTICE.
Sale by Auction of Grown Lands in Large
Portions.
IT is notified for public information that, under
the provisions of “ The Special Sales of Land
Act of 1891,” Country Lands in the Colony of
Queensland, distant not less than twenty miles
from a navigable river, or from a railway, or from
the surveyed line of a railway for the construction
of which money has been raised by loan, may be
offered for Sale by Auction, in portions not exceed-
ing 5120 acres. The upset price will in no case be
less than 10s. per acre.
Tile time for payment may be extended to a
period not exceeding three years, instalments
payable later than six months from the date of
sale bearing interest at 5 per cent, per annum.
Persons desirous of having Crown lands offered
at auction are invited to apply to the Under
Secretary for Public Lands, Brisbane, or any
Crown Lands Commissioner.
A. S. COWLEY,
Secretary for Public Lands.
Department of Public Lands,
P.ri ibanc, 2Sth October, l'S9l.

LAND OF THE CROCODIL.-
I have read several versions of the murder
of Capt. Logan, the Commandant at the Moreton
Bay penal settlement, from 1825 to 1830, but the
true one is in ray possession, in a diary of one of
the convicts engaged in the transaction. This
diary was given to me by the convict’s son. When
Logan’s murder was reported at Limestone (Ips-
wich) the search-party who went out were accom-
panied by Dr. Cowper, the Government health-
officer, who, after examining the remains, gravely
expressed an opinion that “Capt. Logan was
killed by aboriginal weapons ! ” The story is too
long to give in detail in this column, but the fact
is that Capt. Logan was flogged to death by his
own convict servants, or rather they started to
flog him to death, and finding him hard to kill
they finished the work by smashing his head. He
was battered nearly out of resemblance to
humanity and then flung faee-downwards into a
hole a couple of feet deep, one of the
men observing, “ put the looking
down to hell, for that’s where he’s going to !”
The convicts at the settlement manifested insane
joy at the news of the murder, and sang and
hoorayed all night, in defiance of the warders.
Logan’s ferocious cruelty made the history of his
reign a chapter from the Inferno. It was during
Logan’s time that men killed their mates in order
to be taken to Sydney and hanged, and dozens
escaped into the bush to risk all its unknown
terrors and cannibal savages, in preference to
their unendurable servitude. Logan’s body was
taken to Sydney and buried with military
honours, His widow applied time after time in
vain for a, pension, until finally granted an
annuity of ,£7O. The old convict’s diary, the
diary qf the man who helped to flog Logan to
death, is an interesting document.
* * *
A Western squatter, well-known for his
unspeakable meanness, had a manager to whom he
gave the munificent salary of £2OO per annum.
About six inonths ago two commercial travellers
stayed a night at the station, accompanied by
some choice whisky. Under the influence of this
Caledonian nectar the squatter grew somewhat
garrulous and inflated. He told the commercials
that he_ “ paid the highest wages in Queensland,
and I give my manager £4OO per annum ” ! The
manager was listening. Next day he waited on
the boss and said he was tired of station life and
would go down and settle on the coast. In the
meantime he would take the balance of his past
year s salary at the rate of £4OO per annum ! The
squatter laughed derisively. “Very well,” said
the, manager, “ I shall sue you for the amount and
call those two gentlemen to prove from your own
statement that you pay me £4OO salary !” And
after careful but bitter consideration, the squatter
compromised by giving £lOO more than was due
and the manager departed rejoicing.

In the early days, “ old Paddy O’Sullivan,”
M.L.A., a member of Queensland’s first Parlia-
ment, and still an honoured legislator in the
Assembly, took his warehouse trip round the
country with a horse-team and a waggon. One of
his principal debtors was a prominent colonist
> w h° postponed settlement until
W® Bibernian warrior could stand it no longer.
Faddy has a sharp tongue and a sharp pen. He
sat down and wrote some sarcastic verses calcu-
ated to make Mollish “ sit up.” Then he strolled
gently down to Mellish’s office and read the
sparkling satire to the astonished individual him-
self. “ Now,” said P. 0., “unless you pay me in
20 minutes, begorra Oi’ll paste thim verses on
every gum-tree from Mount Hutton to Dalby,
and likewise insert thim in the Dalby Hieeald !”
And M fell over a stool, rushing to get his
cheque-book. Having heard the verses recited by
the genial composer himself, I can appreciate the
frantic desire of M to get the bill settled.
# # *
The idea that Cowley, the Queensland
Minister for Lands, converted Griffith to kanaka
labour is a pure delusion. Cowley has next to
no influence in the Cabinet, and certainly none at
all with Griffith. In any case, whatever he might
say to Sir Samuel on the subject of kanakas
would likely have an effect the reverse of the
intention. The actual reason of the “ten years
extension ” lies chiefly in the misunderstanding
between Sir Samuel and the Labour-party. The
planters declared war against him, and ended
by drinking “Confusion to Griffith” in Mackay
bars and bar-parlours, because he announced his
intention to place some salutary check on their
unconscionable abuses of the Polynesian Act, and
their reward was—the annihilation of the kanaka
traffic ! The Labour-party treated his advances
with suspicion and derision, and he replies with ;
“At least ten years more of kanaka labour !”
This is plainly indicated in the manifesto.
* # #
No man living knows Sir Samuel better
than I do. He is a lonely man, a stranger in the
crowd, a sojourner among his friends, much of
Diogenes, much of Aristophanes, an isolated
planet revolving on his own axis, his light brilliant
without heat, surveying mankind from afar off
through intervening ether colder than ice. His
faith in mankind is small, his contempt boundless.
As a Judge he would be stern as Rhadamanthus,
unsympathetic as the grave. We may debate the
merit of all his services to the country, but they
have taken years from his life, made him pre-
maturely old, and his name is unsullied by even
an enemy’s suspicion that personal gaimever in-
fluenced his legislation or administration. His
energy is perpetual, his mental endurance ap-
parently exhaustless. His friendship is not born
of ardent sympathetic sensibilities, being little
more than an impassioned recognition of merit.
His friend is only a fine picture, a graceful statue.
If he were a bachelor and courting, ho could give
notice of motion that, onacertain day, lie “would
move that, in the opinion of this House, his
personal regard for Miss So-and-So justifies him
in taking formal possession.” His dislikes are
bitter as the waters of Marah. Prom him the
political teleologist retires baffled.
# # *
The first attempt to introduce coolies from
India was in 1838, when G. R. Mayo applied for
a tract of country at Moreton Bay to start a coffee
and cotton plantation. He also asked for a grant
of Eagle Farm, six miles from Brisbane, then the
female convict settlement, and made a proposal to
utilise the women as labourers. In reply to the
coolie question, Lord Normanby wrote to Mayo,
on the 12th of March, 1839, to say that, “ the
introduction of Indian labourers is in direct
opposition to the policy of Her Majesty’s Govern-
ment.”
* * *
How many people know that “Fanny
Bay,” at Palmerston, in the Northern Territory,
was named after Fanny Carandini by the Marine
surveyors ?
Bailey, the Queensland botanist, is a true
democrat. He hates titles and Imperialism like
pizen, and expresses himself on the subject with
great freedom, if necessary. On his recent visit
to South Australia as Queensland representative
at the Science Congress, his democracy came into
collision with the Royalism of Baron Mueller.
The Baron was fussing round saying, “I have just
seen Sir So-and-so.” or Baron this, or C.M.G.
that, “ have you seen him, Mr. Bailey ?” This
became monotonous until Bailey observed “Oh,
damn yoUr titled friends ! I haven’t seen them,
and don’t want to see them. I’m not in the lick-
spittle business; they can go to !” Here
Bailey paused for want of a suitable destination.
The horrified Baron exclaimed “ Ach! mein Gott!”
• # *
The first man who found gold on the
Palmer was surveyor Warner, son of Queensland’s
late Sergeant-at-arms. He discovered it when
passing over the Palmer country with Hann’s
expedition in 1872.
• • *
The first white woman on the Darling
Downs was named Kate M'Ginnis. Her nationality
has never been disputed. She was housekeeper in
1842 on Leslie’s Canning Downs station, now the
property of the Hon. J. D. Macansh. Kate was
an honest, blue-eyed brunette from the south of
Italy, and spoke Irish fluently with a foreign
accent. There is no record to show if she left any
descendants or whether she was the last of the
illustrious house of M‘Ginnis. She had a lonely,
cheerless life in those wild, rough days, but lived
and toiled and soothed many a sick bushman’s
pillow, like other true Australian pioneer women,
and so she shall not vanish trackless into blue
immensity
“ Without the meed of some melodious tear.”

Bulwer’s Parisians : “ Society is a Avail of
very strong masonry. You dash your head
against it—you scatter your brains, and dislodge
a stone. Society smiles in scorn, effaces the stain,
and replaces the stone.”
The latest theory derides the idea that
hypnotic results are brought about through mag-
netic emanations from the body of the operator.
Such results, it is claimed, are really brought
about by tiring a single line of nerve.
The other day a Sydney Technological
Museum official showed a potentate over that
institution for a full hour, under the impression
that he was Minister Suttor. Having pushed
aside everyone else to make room for the all-
important F.B.S., the attendant was paralysed
when the visitor signed himself, on leaving, “John
Blank ” —a Newtown grocer. The cream of the
joke, however, was the matter-of-fact way in
which the august visitor (a J.P.) received the
official's oppressive civilities.

The Laugning Jackass.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When the dew lies deep on the upland lawn.
And the clouds are tipped with gold,
When the first grey gleam of the early dawn
Breaks over the sleeping wold;
Then far and near you will always hear
A voice both loud and gay :
’Tis the little grey bird whose song is heard
A-bidding the dawn good-day.
For he laughs “ Ha, ha,”
And he shouts “Ho, ho,”
Whatever may come to pass ;.
Sure if ever on earth there’s a haven of mirth
’Tis the soul of the laughing-jackass.
When the sun peeps in at the breakfast hour
And rouses the swagsman lone
To find that the ’possum has filched his flour,
Or the cat his mutton-bone ;
Then, close by the way on some ragged spray,
The jolly grey bird is seen
A-splitting his sides, for he seldom hides
The sound of his satire keen.
For he laughs, &c.
When deep in the heart of the pathless b
Some wight has lost his way,
Or down in the creek with a broken hub
The waggoner toils all day ;
Then the little grey bird, you may take my word,
Atop of some neighbouring tree,
Is waking the wood to his mirthful mood—
“ What a jolly good joke,” thinks he.
For he laughs, &c.
So all day long over hills and shawes
The little grey bird will go,
And wherever a man’s in a fix he’ll pause—
Oh, just for a joke, you know ;
And when night time comes, in the leafy gums
He will hide himself from view,
Where he dreams of snakes till the morning breaks,
As most of us sometimes do.
For he laughs, &c.
G. H. MITCHELL.
Writes a correspondent : —
The Bulletin has had a glorious career among the
natives of New Guinea. It was in this way : Last June,
when my mate and I went over, we penetrated very far
into the interior. At a village called Tunora, on the
head of ihe St. Joseph River, we happened to open a
pack in which, among various articles, were some old
Bulletins which we had brought over from Cooktown.
The niggers had apparently never seen paper before, so
they pounced on these Bulletins and admired them so
much that we were enabled to trade them off for three
large bunches.of bananas. The chief and his leading men
carefully removed the covers, which they kept as their
own especial share. The leaves of the paper were made
into cigarettes which they smoked in bamboo pipes, of
course using the native tobacco, which grows luxuriantly
in the interior. Shortly after this we noticed a com-
motion at one end of the village. Lo and behold, the
chief and his men had most artistically decorated their
heads with Bulletin covers.
The Bulletin has a considerable respect
for the wooden part of the Bench, but the meat
part of it is beneath contempt. At Castlemaine
(Vic.) two swagsinen who asked a resident for
something to eat have just been sent to gaol for 12
months. At Bendigo (Vic.) a brothel-keeper and
procuress, who was proved to have girls
of 13 years to her house and lived on the proceeds
of their degradation, was duly convicted before
the local court, and the law, after talking to her
like an outraged parent, gave her a fortnight in
which to clear out of the city. It will probably
cost her about £5 to move her harem to another
town. |

Mrs. P’s Love-Potion.
DRAMATIS PERSON JO :
Mrs. Polly Pdblicou.se, Licensee of the Haivk’s
Inn (buxom, brazen, bewitching).
Jack, or Johnny, a drover—not altogether unknown
to Mrs. P. —going East with cattle.
A blacksmith, bar-loafers, stray dogs, heat, dust and
flies, as required.
Scene. —The bar of the Hawk’s Nest Inn, Paroo
River (W. A.)
(Mrs. P. discovered washing glasses).
Enter Johnny (smiling),
Mrs. P.—
Hullo Johnny, I am pleased to see you’ve come
back,
Johnny—
Burn me, but I’m mightily glad
I didn’t go round by the Hungerford track—■
(Calls to the Groom)
You needn’t unsaddle my prad—
I had half a mind to, they said that a flea
Could be flogged from the Border to here,
But it’s much of a muchness as far as I see—
Mrs. P. (wiping a glass) —
What’s your liquor?
Johnny— I’ll take a small beer,
Just a mouthful to wash the dust out of my
throat —
And, besides, Mrs. P., there was you,
’Twouldn’t do to be near and not call.
Mrs. P. — Jack, you goat,
Make an end of your foolishness, do.
(insinuatingly)
But, Johnny, you don’t mean to say you won’t
stop.
Oh, you must stay and hear all my news.
Johnny—
I must go to the cattle, my men ain’t much chop.
Enter Blacksmith and others.
Blacksmith (with empressement) —
Hul-10, Johnny, old man, have a booze ?
Johnny (hesitatingly) —
Well, Ted, as it’s you, why, um, let it be “Scotch,”
For friendship, I can’t stay to chat.
They’re waiting for me, now, to take the first
watch.
Mrs. P. (aside, viciously) —
Pve a small say, friend Johnny, in that.
(Exit Blacksmith and others. Johnny stoops
down to buckle his spur. Mrs. P. takes
advantage of his inattention, and reaching
down a certain decanter from the top shelf,
places it ready.)
Mrs. P. (softly)—Must you go, Jack 1
JohNNY (avoiding her eye)—
Yes, Polly, I’ve made up my mind.
This gambling and drinking’s all rot —
I’ve put in the peg.
Mrs. P. (crescendo) —Do you fancy I’m blind
Are you going to be married or what ?
Johnny (sullenly) —That’s about it.
Mrs. P. (filling him a glass from (he decanter,
and herself one from the brandy keg, he takes
it and prepares to drink) —
Well, Jack, it’s not worth while to scold,
Though I have been put down by a lass.
Well, here’s to the new love, and let’s drown the
old,
With a laugh for its requiem mass
(they both drink).
Johnny (shuddering and spitting on the floor) —
That whisky’s d d bad, I must go; well,
so’long.
Mrs. P. (coming from behind the bar and looking
into his eyes) —
Do you mind, Jack, our last year’s good-bye?
(Linking her arm in his and leading him off.)
Come into the parlour, I’ll sing you a song
For the last time; it’s no use to cry
Over milk that is spilt or a lover that’s false.
Johnny (putting his hand to his head and
staggering)
Sing, Polly, my head’s in a whirl.
Mrs. P.—
Here sit on this sofa, I’ll play you a waltz
While you dream of your down-country girl.
(Plays a few bars, then sings, mockingly, “ It’s well
to be off with the old love,” <&c., leaves off
abruptly and comes and hangs over the sofa.)
You’ll stay with me now, Jack, you don’t want
to go ?
Johnny ( hoarsely, striving to throw off the lethargy
caused by the drug) —
Yes, d n you, I must and I will.
Hi, Mick, where’s my horse?
Mrs. P. (caressing him) —Hush, Johnny, go slow ;
There, there now, keep quiet, you’re ill.
Johnny (fast losing consciousness) —
Oh, Poll . . didn’t think . . doctor . . grog !
(He sleeps.)
Mrs. P. (meditatively) —
How easily broke is man’s vow,
When a woman so wills it (to him.) Yes, lie like
a log;
Where’s your good resolution, gone now?
(Proceeds to turn out the sleeping man'spockets.)
What’s he worth ? Twenty, thirty, why forty odd
pound—
Oh, Johnny, you ought to know better
Than to carry that on you—now, this, I’ll be
bound,
Is his poor little sweetheart’s last letter.
By the writing, I take it she’s not long from
school.
(Skims the letter, reads the closing lines aloud.)
“It seems such a sad time to wait,
I pray for you often ” — (aside) —you poor little
fool,
“And Jack, darling Jack, do keep straight.”
Here’s her picture. Keep straight ! Would you
pray if you knew
Half as much as I do about Jack ?
Are you honest ? You look it — (bitterly)—so was
I ere I drew
That d d knave of hearts from the pack.
So you love him, well look at him—deaf, dumb,
and blind,
Mine, mine, I say, body and soul.
(Walks up and down the room, holding the money
and portrait in her hand.) s
Will your heart break, I wonder ? I have half a
mind,
(Pauses, looks at the picture, then thrusts it quickly
back in the sleeper's pocket.)
You can have him (looks at the money for some
time, finally thrusts it in beside the girl's
picture) without even toll.
(She throws a rug over the sleeping man, walks to
the back-door of the hotel, and calls the groom
from the stable.)
Ride down to the cattle-camp—take my bay
horse—
It’s somewhere near Henniker’s Ford,
And tell ’em they’d better come after their boss,
For he’s up here, as drunk as a lord.
(The groom docs her bidding, marvelling greatly
the while.)
L’Envoi.
No blade was e’er tempered, that might not be
gapped,
No metal e’er cast, but had some little flaw,
No halter e’er plaited, that might not be snapped,
No heart, but may somehow bo touched on the
raw.
Tropical Cuffers.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A droving trip from Queensland to Vic-
toria may seem a gigantic undertaking to many,
but when one knows that every mob of cattle is
accompanied by several stockmen, a black boy, a
dray, or plenty of pack-horses, not forgetting a
cook to look after the creature comforts of the
men, while the route is along a well-beaten_ track,
such an affair is more like a prolonged picnic than
a wearisome journey. In the face of a mustering
and droving feat done by two men, L and
W , in the early days of the Palmer, the
droving of these times looks insignificant. At
that period cattle were worth from £l2 to ,£l5 per
head on the diggings, and the man who could
safely deliver a mob was certain to make a “ rise.”
This is what L and W were after,
and they saw their opportunity. All the property
they had in the world was a saddle-horse
and saddle and bridle apiece, but not a
cent of money. It was the middle of the
wet season when overseers and stockmen were
cowering in their huts, not caring to face the
pitiless rain and the no less pitiless bog. Anyone
who has been through a Northern wet season
knows what the ground is like after a few weeks’
rain : every step a horse takes puts him “knees
under.” Marsh flies and sand flies devour one in
the day-time, and mosquitoes at night. But L
and W were going for a “rise,” and a rise
they meant to make. Recollect there were but
two of them, without pack-horses or any means of
carrying food, except what they could stow on
their saddles, and they were actually destitute of
blankets. Dalgonelly Station, on the Flinders,
was GOO miles or more from their market (the
Palmer), but there were some nice bullocks on
Dalgonelly—just the ones to command a good
price. To Dalgonelly L and partner accord-
ingly went, mustered 60 head of fats, and drove
them the whole distance thence to the Palmer,
never losing a hoof and going, not along a
main road, but through the trackless bush. How
did they manage to live during the time ?
They ate the tails off the bullocks. They could
not afford to kill one, and had no salt to cure it
even had they done so. Unfortunately, their
pluck did not meet with its reward, but their
rascality did. They were annexed by the police
after they had sold the bullocks, but before they
got the money.
# # •
Many anecdotes are related of old
MTntyre, owner of Dalgonelly, who, having the
bump of locality but poorly developed, was notorious
for his bad bushmanship. He left home one
morning on a long journey, and during the day
his horse slewed round while old Mac. was deep
in thought, and took him right home again whe
he arrived about sundown. As Mao. rode un f 6
the homestead, he said to a man he met “r
thought there was something familiar about the 1
buildings.” ese
* * *
Mac. went down to be married once and
drove his wife back to the station in a bugm/
One evening he left Madame in charge of th
vehicle while he went off the road in search f
water—and got thoroughly bushed. His wif
passed a miserable night sitting in the buggy u. 8
mosquitoes eating slabs out of her, and liangi n Q
on to a pair of restless horses, who naturally wer*
indignant at not being let loose. Mac. returned
to the buggy at daylight after wandering abom
all night, and told his wife that she ought alwav”
light a fire when a man was away from camp at
night. Not long after this Mac. got lost on th
run, and as he did not turn up at night fiia
wife became very anxious, but, remembering her
instructions, put a lighted candle in every window
of the house, and caused numerous fires to be
lighted round about. At midnight Mac’s horsn
brought his tired rider within sight of the ill u .
ruinations. “ Big camp of niggers, by g llni I>i
muttered Mac. as he sneaked carefully away in an
opposite direction.
# * *
Mac aforesaid was an original old chap
his way. He once sent a mob of fats to the
Palmer in the charge of his overseer, who made a
very bad bargain with the butchers. Mac. heard
of this ere the overseer’s return and when the
culprit arrived was waiting for him on the
verandah with his cheque made up to date stuck
on the end of a pole thirty-feet long, which ho
poked at him in gloomy silence.
In Melbourne the other clay a man was
arrested in a hideous state of alcoholism and
remanded for three days for “ medical treatment.”
At the end of that period he came up again and
entered a powerful protest in court. He said
that he had got no medical treatment—some
coarse policeman had pumped water on him and
given him a big spoonful of salts, and that was all.
Justice travels fast in New Guinea. A
naked aboriginal over there committed, or was
alleged to have committed, a murder on Christmas
Eve; he was tried under a spreading tree on
Christmas morning, and hanged on a Xmas tree
the same day. This was the same offender who
according to the theory of Sir Samuel Griffith'
probably rather liked being hanged on account of
the scenery. As Griffith himself was part of the
scenery, he probably did.
Those who are inquisitive about what
“Rolf Boldrewood ” makes or ought to make
by his books may make a calculation for
themselves on the following basis. Accord-
ing to one of the best-known publishers,
300 copies of a three-volume novel, published
at 31s. (id., will about pay expenses. After
that profits commence. Of a six-shilling edition
—which means about 4s. Gd. cash, retail—OOOOcopies
must go off to clear cost. Now, Rolf’s “Robbery
Under Arms ” has already had a sale of 40,000,
and his other works have been in almost equal
demand. They are published nominally at 65.,
but the retail price now is 3s. At this sum, even,
the figures tot up handsomely. “ Robbery Under
Arms ” is the only one of his that was published
in 3-vol. form at 31s. Gd., and there can be no
doubt that at least 1000 were sold at that nrice.

Years Ago.
The old dead flowers of bygone summers,
The old sweet songs that are no more sung,
The rose-red dawns that were welcome comers
When you and I and the world were young,
Are lost, O love, to the light for ever,
And seen no more of the moon or sun,
For seas divide, and the seasons sever,
And twain are we that of old were one.
O fair lost love, when the ship went sailing
Across the seas in the years agone,
And seaward-set were the eyes unquailing,
And land ward-looking the faces wan
My heart went back as a dove goes homeward
With wings aweary to seek its nest,
While fierce sea-eagles are flying foamward
And storm-winds whiten the surge’s crest.
And far inland for a farewell pardon
Flew on and on, while the ship went South,
The rose was red in the red-rose garden,
And red the rose of your laughing mouth.
But no word came on the wind in token
-Of love that lasts till the end ; and so
My heart returned to me bruised and broken,
Frqju you, my love, of the long ago.
The green fields seemed in the distance growing
To silken squares on a weaver’s loom,
As oversea came the land wind blowing
The faint sweet scent of the clover bloom.
A rarer odour to me it carried,
In subtle delicate way to tell
Of you, ere you and the world were married—
The lilac-odour you loved so well.
Again, I saw you beneath the blooms of
Those lilac-trees in the garden old.
Ah me ! each tree is a mark for tombs of
Dead dreams and memories still and cold.
And Death comes there with his breath scent-laden,
And gath’ring gently the blossoms shed
(In guise of Autumn, the brown-browed maiden),
With your and my dead buries his dead.
O, fairer far than the fair ideal
Of him who imaged the foam-born Queen
In foam-white marble- a dream made real—
To me were you in those years, I ween.
Your lips were redder than night-shade berries
That burn in borders of hedgerowed lanes,
And sweeter far than the sweet wild cherries
The June sun flushes with crimson stains.
And gray your eyes as a cuckoo’s wings were—
A gray soft shadowing deeps profound.
Where thoughts that reached to the4ieart of things were,
And love lay dreaming though seeming drowmd.
Twin-tulip breasted like her the tread of
Whose feet made music in Paphos fair,
The world to me was not worth a thread of
Your brown, ambrosial, braided hair.
Mayhap you loved me at one time truly,
And I was jealous and you were proud,
But mine the love of the king in Thule,
Till death , and yours—sleeps well in shroud.
So night came down like a sombre raven,
And southward ever the ship sailed on,
Till glad green fields and lessening haven
Grew faint and faded like ghosts at dawn.
As fields of Heaven eternal blooming,
Those flowerful fields of my English land,
In midnight visions are still perfuming
All wild waste places and seas of sand.
And still in seasons of storm and thunder,
In strange lands under your land and mine ;
And though our ways have been wide asunder,
In calm and tempest and shade and shine,
Your face I see as I saw the last time—
As one borne space-ward on wings of light,
With eyes turned back to a sight of past time,
Beholds for ever that self-same sight.
But scorn has died on your lips, and through you
Shines out star bright an immortal grace,
As though God then to His heaven drew you,
And sent an angel to take your place.
I plucked one rose from the tree you cherished,
My heart's blood ebbing has kept it red,
And all my hopes with its scent have perished ;
Why mourn them now'—are Hie dead not dead ?
- And yet, God knows, as this rose I kiss, you
May feel the kisses across the sea ;
And soul to soul for the larger issue,
Your soul may stand with the soul of me,
Unknown to you for the strings of Being
Are not so easily snapped or torn ;
And we may journey with eyes unseeing
On paths that meet in the years unborn.
Farewell, dear love. Warm sighs may sever
Ripe lips of love like a rose-leaf curled.
But you remain unto me for ever
The one fair woman in all the world.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

illy appeared, we believe, in the Sydney Mail.
The Age may eventually succeed in work-
ing the Melbourne people into a mild frenzy on
the gas monopoly question, but it will be a long
job, anyway. Besides, the gasmen attached to
that journal are not sufficiently 'em-lung with
their figures and comparisons. “In England,’
says the Age, “the usual system adopted is to
regulate the price of gas in the inverse ratio to the
dividends paid by the different companies.”
Why, the Melbourne Met. Go. makes a faint
pretence of doing that, and is by charter precluded
from paying its highest dividends when the article
Is at its highest price, otherwise the ghastly im-
position would produce 25 percent, as regular as
clockwork. The point to be insisted upon is that
no London gas company has for years past been
licensed to pay more t urn 10 per cent, to share
holders, and there the price of illumination is just
half as much as in Melbourne, where the
monopolists scorn the thought of a dividend under
2L per cent.
The U.S. Government paleontologist is
setting up his collection of gigantic fossil
mammals, birds and reptiles, some of which latter
run to 90ft. in length and must in life have
weighed 30 tons. The hypsirhopus was 30ft. long,
walked erect upon its hind legs and defended itself
by wagging its tail (armed with tremendous
spines) and grasped with its forearms. Its whole
body was sheathed in tremendous armour. Then
there was the agathaumas, whose skull was 25
feet long, and had a huge frill of solid bone six
feet in width extending backward from its head;
this being required for the attachment of tremen-
dous muscles necessary to hold up the skull. It
stood as high as a big elephant and was twice
as long. These two, however, were small com-
pared with the amazing amphicoelias, which was
15 feet high and 60 feet long, and weighed 20 tons.
Each of its footprints covered a square-yard. Its
remains are found where it got stuck in the mud,
which subsequently turned to stone and preserved
it. Put even it was diminutive compared with the
mighty atlantosaur, probably the biggest land-
animal that ever lived, with a thigh-bone eight feet
long. Ichthyosaurs grew to the length of 40 feet, and
had alligator-heads and huge eyes with which to
see into the water-abysses. In a mould made for
a cast of one 26 men comfortably sat down to
dinner. Not less weird than the landscape inthos 6
days must have been the aerial scene, the heaven 8
above the floods being filled with actual dragons
having a 25ft. spread.

A Queensland storekeeper has Just been
convicted of sheep-stealing. From shop-keeping
to sheep-copping is an easy transition.
The other night we turned up a speech by
Governor Loftus, in the Soudan time, in which
the old gentleman gushed over “ the happy time
he had spent in Australia.” Poor old fellow, he
had to do it! What his real feelings about Aus-
tralia were was shown by what he said to a Mel-
bourne Government official who offered him a
calf-bound copy of a State publication issued
under his (the official’s) supervision, and promised
to continue to forward it periodically to Europe.
“ My dear sir,” said the old gentleman bitterly,
“ I have been so unhappy in Australia that I hope
to forget it utterly. I don’t want your book, and
I do not mean to be rude when I say that I hope
never to see it, or anything else Australian again.”
Judging from current symptoms, the good-
hearted donkey who pays half his income to the
Melbourne weeklies for the insertion of a sermon
in each issue will draw upon the discourses of
Rev. John M'Neill for the material hitherto sup-
plied by Spurgeon. M‘Neill began life as a rail-
way-porter. He was once nominated for the
vacant pastorate in a fashionable Presbyterian
Church in a Melbourne suburb—but theportership
stuck in the gizzards of the congregation who had
made their money out of tallow and inflated land-
boom values. M‘Neill succeeded Oswald Dykes
at the wealthiest London chapel of the denomina-
tion —which would seem to show that the British
“ unco guid ” are not such snobs as their
“colonial ” brethren.

One of the curious things to be se c n in a
Australian Metropolitan Cemetery is an awni
covered, iron-railed grave-plot, furnished witl* 1 ”'
seat, small table and locker. It is attended dVl” 1
(wet or fine) by a middle-aged lady, w G* y
parents, husband, and brothers are interred in tl
spot. She has two meals a day in her singul-
tent—breakfast and dinner. She leaves at f 0 ar
o’clock, tile trustees having formally refused h
permission to stay in the cemetery overnight*'
Having some money, she has numerous would-1
heirs who have tried unavailiugly to lock her u°
on the ground of mental incapacity. She defeat J?
them chiefly owing to her production of a neatly
kept cash-book in which her expenditure
detailed to a penny. She tips the sextons
ally, and apparently her only amusement is f
watch the interment of children. When a bio-
coffin heaves in sight, she’s up from her seat like o
shot, and, locking the gate after her, follows the
procession and stays out the service. She never
laugbs nor weeps—but sings frequently to herself
in a low voice.
